Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Seeking Shuf'ah Cancels Khiyar al-Shart
Ruling with proof on shuf'ah canceling khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that seeking shuf'ah during khiyar al-shart cancels the option and explain the proof based on istihsan.

Core Idea
Seeking shuf'ah proves permanent ownership intent, canceling the option retroactively.

This lesson examines a subtle intersection of two key Hanafi doctrines: the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) and the stipulated option (khiyar al-shart). The passage from Al-Hidayah addresses whether a buyer who exercises shuf'ah during the option period still retains the right to cancel the original sale. The ruling clarifies that the act of seeking shuf'ah constitutes implicit consent, thereby extinguishing the option and confirming ownership from the outset.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the fact pattern: a buyer purchases a house with a khiyar al-shart (option condition), then during the option period, an adjacent house is sold to a third party. The buyer claims shuf'ah on the adjacent house.
The option is typically for three days in the Hanafi school.
2
State the Ruling
The Hanafi ruling: seeking shuf'ah during the option period is an expression of consent to the original sale, which cancels the option and establishes ownership from the time of purchase.
The key inference is that shuf'ah is only valid for a permanent neighbor; hence, claiming it implies the buyer treats the first house as permanently owned.
3
Analyze the Proof (Dalil)
The proof is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The purpose of shuf'ah is to remove harm from a new neighbor, which requires permanent ownership of the adjacent property. By seeking shuf'ah, the buyer demonstrates a desire for permanent ownership, which is incompatible with retaining an option. Therefore, the option must have fallen before the shuf'ah claim.
This is a rational inference (dalil aqli) rather than a direct textual proof.
4
Understand the Madhhab Specificity
This exposition is specifically needed for Imam Abu Hanifa's school because he holds that khiyar al-shart is included within the buyer's ownership. Under this view, the buyer can validly claim shuf'ah, but doing so triggers the cancellation of the option. Other schools may differ on whether shuf'ah can be claimed during the option period or whether it implies consent.
The passage emphasizes that the reasoning is tailored to Abu Hanifa's framework.
5
Apply the Ruling
In practice, a buyer who wants to preserve the option should avoid exercising shuf'ah until the option expires. If they do exercise it, they lose the right to cancel the original purchase.
This has practical implications for property transactions.

This passage presents a ruling that when a buyer of a house (under khiyar al-shart) exercises the right of shuf'ah (pre-emption) for an adjacent property during the option period, his action is deemed consent to the original purchase, thereby canceling the option and retroactively confirming ownership from the time of purchase. This reasoning is specifically required for Abu Hanifa's view that the option is part of the buyer's ownership, so the buyer can validly claim shuf'ah. The passage clarifies the legal logic and the Hanafi position.

خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): Option stipulated in the contract allowing cancellation within a specified period.
شفعہ (shuf'ah): Right of pre-emption for an adjacent property to prevent harm from a new neighbor.

Two Buyers with Khiyar: One Consents, Other's Right to Return
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on effect of one buyer's consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding khiyar when one of two joint buyers consents, and explain the legal reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Whether one buyer's consent cancels the other's khiyar depends on whether khiyar is a shared or individual right.

When two buyers jointly purchase an item with khiyar (option to rescind), and one of them consents, does the other lose his right to return? This classical Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (the Sahibayn) turns on a fundamental question: is the khiyar a single right held jointly, or two independent rights? The answer affects not only khiyar al-shart but also khiyar al-'ayb (defect) and khiyar al-ru'yah (sight). In this lesson, we will dissect both proofs and see how principles of avoiding harm (darar) and individual right ownership interact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal issue
Two buyers each have khiyar (option) in a joint purchase. One buyer consents to the sale. Does the other buyer lose his right to rescind? The difference hinges on the nature of khiyar: joint or individual.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the other buyer cannot return the item. His proof: the item left the seller's ownership free of the 'defect of partnership'. If one buyer returns it, the seller would receive a share in the item (since the consenting buyer still owns half), causing additional harm (darar zā'id). Khiyar is not meant to cause harm.
The defect of partnership is a legal construct: returning only part of the item imposes a new liability on the seller.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the other buyer retains his khiyar and can return his share. Their proof: khiyar is established for each buyer individually (li kull wāḥid minhumā). One's act of consent cannot nullify the other's independent right because that would void his right (ibṭāl ḥaqqihī).
They emphasize individual entitlement: the khiyar is not shared but separate.
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
Under Abu Hanifa, once one buyer consents, the sale becomes binding on both. Under the Sahibayn, the non-consenting buyer can still rescind. This difference applies also to khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ru'yah.
The same legal reasoning extends to other khiyar types, showing the principle's breadth.
5
Determine the muftā bihī (operative position)
The passage does not explicitly give tarjīḥ. Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Haskafī, Ibn 'Ābidīn) tend to prefer the Sahibayn's view because it preserves individual rights and avoids nullifying a khiyar without clear cause. However, both positions are valid within the madhhab.
Students should be aware that the dispute remains classical and both views may be relied upon depending on context.

Source Text

دو آدمیوں نے ایک غلام خرید دونوں کو خیار تھا ایک راضی ہو گیا تو دوسرا رد کر سکتا ہے یا نہیں ، اقوال فقہاء قال واذا اشترى الرجلان غلاما على انهما بالخيار فرضى احدهما فليس للاخران يرده عندابي حنيفة وقالا له ان يرده وعلى هذا الخلاف خيار العيب وخيار الروية لهما ان اثبات الخيار لهما اثباته لكل واحد منهما فلا يسقط باسقاط صاحبه لمافيه من ابطال حقه وله ان المبيع خرج عن ملكه غير معيب بعيب الشركة فلورده احدهمـارده مـعـیـبـا بـه وفيه الزام ضرر زائد وليس من ضرورة اثبات الخيار ولهما الرضاء برد احدهما لتصور اجتماعهما على الرد ترجمہ جامع صغیر میں کہا کہ جب دو آدمیوں نے ایک غلام اس شرط پر خریدا کہ ان دونوں کو خیار شرط حاصل ہے۔ پھر ان دونوں میں سے ایک راضی ہو گیا تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک دوسرے کو واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہے۔ اور اسی اختلاف پر خیار عیب اور خیار رؤیت ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ دونوں کیلئے خیار ثابت کرنا ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کے واسطے خیار ثابت کرنا ہے۔ اسلئے اس کے ساتھی کے خیار ساقط کرنے سے اس کا خیار ساقط نہ ہو گا۔ کیونکہ اس میں اس کے حق کا باطل کرنا ہے اور امام صاحب کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بائع کی ملک سے مبیع ایسی حالت میں نکلی کہ اس میں شرکت کا عیب نہیں تھا۔ پس اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک اس کو رد کر دے تو اس کو عیب شرکت کے ساتھ عیب دار کر کے واپس کرے گا ۔ حالانکہ اس میں ضر زائد کا الزام ہے اور دونوں کیلئے خیار ثابت کرنے سے لازم نہیں آتا ہے کہ وہ ان میں سے ایک کے راضی کرنے پر راضی ہو کیونکہ دونوں کو واپس کرنے پر متفق ہونا ممکن ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ دو شخصوں نے ایک غلام اس شرط کے ساتھ خریدا کہ دونوں کیلئے خیار شرط ہے۔ پھر ان دونوں میں سے ایک بیج پر راضی ہو گیا تو حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک دوسرے مشتری کا خیار باطل ہو گیا اس کو اپنے خیار کے تحت بیع رد کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ اس کا خیار باقی ہے اس کو اپنے خیار کے تحت بیع رد کرنے کا پورا پورا اختیار ہے۔ یہی اختلاف خیار عیب اور خیار رویت میں ہے۔ خیار عیب کی صورت یہ ہے کہ دو شخصوں نے ایک چیز بغیر دیکھے خریدی پھر دیکھ کر ایک راضی ہو گیا تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک دوسرا مشتری خیار رویت کی وجہ سے اس کو واپس نہیں کر سکتا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک واپس کر سکتا ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ دونوں مشتریوں کیلئے خیار کا اثبات در حقیقت ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کیلئے اثبات ہے۔ اور جو خیار دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کیلئے ثابت ہو اس کو اگر ایک نے ساقط کر دیا تو اس سے دوسرے کا خیار ساقط نہیں ہوتا۔ کیونکہ اس میں اس دوسرے مشتری کے حق کو باطل کرنا لازم آتا ہے اور دوسرے کے حق کو باطل کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے صرف ایک کے خیار ساقط کرنے سے دوسرے کا خیار ساقط نہ ہوگا۔ حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مبیع بائع کی ملکیت سے ایسی حالت میں نکلی ہے کہ اس میں شرکت کا عیب نہیں تھا۔ اب اگر ان دونوں مشتریوں میں سے فقط ایک اس کو واپس کرے تو ایسی حالت میں واپس کرے گا کہ اس میں عیب شرکت ہے یعنی ایک مشتری ( جس نے بیج رو نہیں کی) اور بائع کے درمیان بیع مشترک ہو گئی۔ حالانکہ اس صورت میں بائع پر ضرر زائد لازم آتا ہے اور اپنے عمل سے
Two men bought a slave with khiyar. One was satisfied, so can the other return? Statements of jurists: He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: When two men bought a slave on condition that both have khiyar, then one of them was satisfied, then according to Abu Hanifa the other does not have the right to return it, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he has the right to return it. And on this same difference are khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ru'yah. Their (Sahibayn's) proof is that establishing khiyar for both means establishing it for each one of them, so it does not fall by the dropping of his companion, because that would nullify his right. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that the sold item left the seller's ownership without the defect of partnership. So if one of them returns it, he returns it with the defect of partnership, and that involves imposing additional harm, and that is not necessitated by establishing khiyar. And the two (Sahibayn) (say) that the satisfaction of one of them with the return is not (required) because it is possible for both to agree on the return. Translation: It is said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: When two men bought a slave on condition that both have khiyar al-shart, then one of them was satisfied, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the other does not have the option to return it, and according to the Sahibayn he has the right to return it. And on this same difference are khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ru'yah. The Sahibayn's proof is that establishing khiyar for both means establishing it for each one of them, so it does not fall by the dropping of his companion, because that would nullify his right. And Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the sold item left the seller's ownership without the defect of partnership. So if one of them returns it, he returns it with the defect of partnership, and that involves imposing additional harm, and that is not necessitated by establishing khiyar. And (for the Sahibayn) it is possible for both to agree on the return. Explanation: The scenario is that two persons bought a slave with khiyar al-shart for both. Then one of them was satisfied with the sale. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the other buyer's option becomes void, and he does not have the right to rescind. The Sahibayn said his option remains, and he has full right to rescind. The same difference applies to khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ru'yah. For khiyar al-'ayb, two persons buy an item without seeing, then after seeing one is satisfied; according to Abu Hanifa the other cannot return it, according to the Sahibayn he can. The Sahibayn's proof is that khiyar is established for each buyer individually; one's dropping does not nullify the other's. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the item left the seller without the defect of partnership; if only one returns it, it returns with the defect of partnership, causing additional harm to the seller.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding khiyar when there are two buyers. The issue is whether one buyer's satisfaction (acceptance) terminates the other buyer's khiyar. Abu Hanifa holds that it does, because if only one returns the item, it would impose a defect (partial ownership) on the seller. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that each buyer's khiyar is independent; one's acceptance does not affect the other's option. The same disagreement applies to khiyar al-'ayb (option for defect) and khiyar al-ru'yah (option after seeing). The passage provides both sides' proofs and explains the scenario, emphasizing the practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the second buyer loses his right to return; under the Sahibayn, he retains it.

Key Terms
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option to return a purchased item due to a defect.
خیار رؤیت (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option to return an item after seeing it if purchase was without inspection.
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): Option to rescind a sale by stipulation within a specified period.
ضرر زائد (ḍarar zā'id): Additional harm beyond what is normally tolerable in contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why does one buyer's satisfaction not cancel the other buyer's khiyar?
A) Because khiyar is indivisible
B) Because khiyar is established individually for each
C) Because the seller agrees
D) Because the item is not yet owned

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when two buyers have independent khiyar, one buyer's consent does not necessarily cancel the other's option; the dispute hinges on whether khiyar is a joint or individual right.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Joint Buyers with Khiyar: Does One's Acceptance Extinguish the Other's Option?
Intra-madhhab dispute on khiyar when one joint buyer accepts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position and intra-madhhab dispute regarding khiyar al-shart when one of two joint buyers accepts, and apply the concept of 'ayb al-shirkah to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa extinguishes joint khiyar after one acceptance; Sahibayn preserve individual rights.

When two buyers jointly purchase an item with a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart), and one buyer decides to keep the item, does the other buyer still have the right to return it? This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The disagreement hinges on whether khiyar is a single right shared by the buyers or an individual right for each, and whether allowing one buyer's rejection would cause unjustified harm to the seller. We will explore the proofs and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disagreement
Two men buy a slave with khiyar al-shart for both. One becomes satisfied; the other wishes to exercise his khiyar and return the item. Does he have that right?
This is the core factual setting.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the second buyer loses the right to reject. His proof: the item left the seller's ownership free from any defect. If only one buyer returns it, the item returns with a 'defect of partnership' (عيب الشركة) because the seller becomes co-owner with the one who accepted. This imposes additional harm on the seller, which is not justified.
The harm is 'additional' because the seller did not consent to partial return.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn hold that the second buyer retains the full right to reject. Their proof: khiyar is established for each individual buyer. One buyer's dropping of his right cannot nullify the other's established right.
They treat khiyar as a bundle of individual rights.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response to the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa's supporters respond: the seller's consent to the sale was on the condition that both buyers either accept together or reject together. The possibility of joint rejection exists, so the seller never agreed to partial rejection. Hence, establishing khiyar for both does not imply the seller must accept partial return.
The response is that the seller's presumed consent is limited.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if one buyer agrees to the sale, according to Abu Hanifa the other cannot return the item; according to the Sahibayn he can. In later Hanafi practice, the view of the Sahibayn is often preferred for fatwa.
This affects joint purchases with return options, e.g., buying a car or property with a cooling-off period.

کیلئے ثابت ہو اس کو اگر ایک نے ساقط کر دیا تو اس سے دوسرے کا خیار ساقط نہیں ہوتا۔ کیونکہ اس میں اس دوسرے مشتری کے حق کو باطل کرنا لازم آتا ہے اور دوسرے کے حق کو باطل کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے صرف ایک اسلئے مقید کیا ہے کہ رد کے ممتنع ہونے کی صورت میں اس مشترکی کا نقصان ہوگا جو بیچ پر راضی نہیں ہوا بلکہ اس کو رد کرنا چاہتا ہے مگر ربیع کے ممتنع ہونے کی صورت میں اس مشترکی کا نقصان کم ہے اور رد کرنے کی صورت میں بائع کا نقصان زیادہ ہے۔ اسلئے بائع کے نقصان کا لحاظ کرتے ہوئے واپس کرنے کی اجازت نہیں دی گئی ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل کا جواب ..... وليس من ضرورة ... الخ سے صاحبین کی دلیل کا جواب ہے۔ جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ دونوں مشتریوں کو خیار دینے سے یہ لازم نہیں آتا کہ بائع ان دونوں میں سے ایک کے واپس کرنے پر بھی راضی ہو۔ کیونکہ دونوں کا متفق ہو کر واپس کرنا ممکن ہے۔ پس بائع اس بات پر راضی ہوا تھا کہ دونوں مل کر بیع پوری کریں یا دونوں متفق ہو کر واپس کریں۔ اس پر قطعا راضی نہیں ہوا تھا کہ ایک بیج کو پوری کرے اور دوسرا رد کرے۔
He said: And if two men buy a slave on the condition that both have the option, and one of them is pleased, then for the other there is no right to return it according to Abu Hanifa, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He has the right to return it. And upon this difference is the option of defect and the option of seeing. For them (the Sahibayn): establishing the option for them is establishing it for each one of them, so it does not fall by the fall of his companion because that would nullify his right. For him (Abu Hanifa): The sold item left the seller's ownership without the defect of partnership; if one of them returns it, he returns it defective with that defect, and in that is imposing additional harm, and it is not necessitated by establishing the option. And for them (the Sahibayn): The seller is pleased with the return of one of them because it is conceivable they agree on the return. Translation of Jami' al-Saghir: When two men buy a slave on condition that both have khiyar al-shart, then one of them becomes satisfied, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the other does not have the right to return it. And upon this difference are khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ru'yah. The Sahibayn's proof: Establishing khiyar for both is establishing it for each one of them, so if one drops his khiyar, the other's khiyar does not drop because that would nullify his right. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: The mabi' left the seller's ownership in a state free from the defect of shirkah (partnership); if one of them returns it, he returns it with the defect of shirkah, and that imposes additional harm on the seller. And establishing khiyar for both does not necessitate that the seller is pleased with the return by one of them, because it is possible for them to agree on the return together. Explanation: The scenario is that two persons buy a slave with khiyar al-shart for both. Then one of them becomes pleased with the sale. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the other buyer's khiyar becomes void; he has no right to reject. The Sahibayn say his khiyar remains; he has full right to reject. This difference applies to khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ru'yah. The Sahibayn argue that khiyar established for both is actually established for each individually; one's dropping does not affect the other's. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the slave left the seller's ownership without the defect of partnership; if only one returns it, he returns it with that defect, causing extra harm to the seller. The seller only agreed to the sale on the understanding that both would either complete the sale together or return it together, not that one would complete and the other return.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-'ayb, and khiyar al-ru'yah when two persons jointly purchase an item. The core issue: if one of the two buyers becomes satisfied with the purchase, does the other retain his right to exercise khiyar and return the item? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the second buyer loses his khiyar; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that he retains it. The Sahibayn reason that khiyar is established for each buyer individually, so one's acceptance cannot nullify the other's right. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is based on the concept of 'defect of partnership' (عيب الشركة): returning the item by only one buyer would impose a new defect on the item (partial ownership), causing additional harm to the seller. Since the seller did not consent to such partial return, the khiyar is extinguished once one buyer accepts. The same principle applies to khiyar al-'ayb (option due to defect) and khiyar al-ru'yah (option after seeing). The commentary clarifies that the seller's harm in case of rejection is greater than the buyer's loss in being unable to reject, hence his protection is prioritized. The Sahibayn's argument is refuted by stating that khiyar does not entail the seller's agreement to partial rejection.

Key Terms
خیار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): Option stipulated in the contract allowing the buyer(s) to cancel within a specified period.
خیار العیب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option to return an item due to a defect present at the time of sale unknown to the buyer.
خیار الرویة (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option to cancel the sale after seeing an item bought sight unseen.
عيب الشركة ('ayb al-shirkah): Defect of partnership arising when only one of two joint buyers returns the item, creating partial ownership for the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), if one of two joint buyers accepts the sale during the khiyar period, what happens to the other buyer's khiyar?
A) It is extinguished because the khiyar is a single shared right.
B) It remains because khiyar is established for each buyer individually.
C) It is automatically converted into khiyar al-'ayb.
D) It becomes dependent on the seller's consent.

Key Takeaway
In joint purchases with khiyar, Abu Hanifa extinguishes the khiyar upon one buyer's acceptance to prevent harm to the seller; the Sahibayn preserve individual khiyar rights.

Khiyar al-Wasf: Option When a Stipulated Description Is Missing
Ruling on khiyar al-wasf with talil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's options under khiyar al-wasf and explain why the contract remains valid despite the missing description.

Core Idea
When a stipulated desirable description is missing, the buyer cancels or takes at full price—no reduction allowed.

This passage from al-Hidayah deals with a common scenario: buying a slave (or any item) with a specific skill stipulated, only to find the skill absent. The Hanafi ruling provides the buyer with a choice—to take the item as is or cancel—but crucially, the contract is not automatically void. Why? Because the missing description does not change the essential type of the item. Understanding this principle helps distinguish between essential and non-essential attributes in sale contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize the scenario: a slave is sold with a stipulation that he is a baker or scribe, but he lacks that skill.
The description must be desirable (wasf marghub fih) and explicitly stipulated.
2
State the ruling
The buyer has khiyar al-wasf: an option to either take the slave for the full price or to cancel the sale.
The contract is not automatically void; the buyer has a choice.
3
Understand the reasoning
The description is desirable, so stipulation creates an entitlement; its loss gives the buyer an option because his consent was conditional on that description.
The text says: 'لانه ما رضى به دونه' – he was not satisfied without it.
4
Analyze why contract is not void
The missing description does not change the essential type (naw') of the item, similar to gender in animals or the description of soundness.
The analogy shows that only essential changes invalidate the contract.
5
Note the price consequence
The buyer takes the item at the full price because descriptions are subsidiary and not allocated any portion of the price.
The text: 'الاوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن'.

Source Text

He said: And if someone sells a slave on condition that he is a baker or a scribe, but he turns out to be contrary to that, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes him for the full price, and if he wishes, he leaves him. And because this is a desirable description, so it becomes entitled in the contract by the condition; then its loss necessitates giving the option because he was not satisfied without it. And this reverts to a difference in type due to the slight variation in preferences, so the contract does not become void by its absence, like the description of male and female in animals. And it became like the loss of the description of soundness. And if he takes him, he takes him for the full price because descriptions are not met by any portion of the price, as they are subsidiary in the contract, as is known. Translation: He said: And if someone sells a slave on condition that he is a baker or a scribe, but he turns out to be contrary to that, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he takes him for the full price, and if he wishes, he leaves him. Because this is a desirable description, so in the contract of sale, by stipulating it, it becomes entitled. Then the loss of that description necessitates giving the buyer an option because he was not satisfied with the sale without that description. And this reverts to a difference in type due to the slight variation in preferences, so the contract does not become void by its absence (like) the description of male and female in animals. And it became like the loss of the description of soundness. And when he takes the sold item, he takes it for the full price because descriptions are not met by any portion of the price, as descriptions are subsidiary in the contract, as has been known previously. Explanation: The scenario is that if a person buys a slave on condition that he is skilled in baking or knows writing, but it turns out he knows neither baking nor writing, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes to take the slave, he takes him for the full price; if he wishes to reject, he cancels the sale. The proof is that the skills of baking and writing are desirable descriptions, and stipulating a desirable description in the contract of sale...
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling on khiyar al-wasf (option due to missing desirable description) in a sale. The ruling: if a person buys a slave with a stipulation that the slave is a baker or scribe, and the slave lacks that qualification, the buyer has the option to either take the slave for the full price (i.e., waive the condition) or to cancel the sale. The contract is not void because the missing description does not affect the essence of the contract; it is similar to stipulating gender (male/female) in animals — the contract remains valid, and the buyer gets an option. The reasoning: the description is subsidiary, not part of the price; therefore, no portion of the price is allocated to it. The buyer's option arises because his consent was tied to that description. The commentary explains that the description is 'desirable' (مرغوب فيه), and its stipulation creates an entitlement; its loss gives the buyer the choice to accept or reject without adjusting the price, since descriptions are not evaluated separately in the price.

Key Terms
خیار الوصف (khiyar al-wasf): Option for the buyer when a stipulated desirable description is found to be absent.
وصف مرغوب فيه (wasf marghub fih): A description that is desirable and sought after, such as being a baker or scribe.
اختلاف النوع (ikhtilaf al-naw'): Difference in type; here it means that the absence of the description does not change the essential type of the item, so the contract remains valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer can demand a reduced price.
B) The buyer can take the slave for the full price or cancel the sale.
C) The sale is automatically void.
D) The buyer must accept the slave at full price with no option.

Key Takeaway
When a desirable description is stipulated and absent, the buyer may take the item at full price or cancel, but cannot demand a reduction because descriptions are subsidiary.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Khiyar for Missing Stipulated Description (Slave as Baker or Scribe)
Ruling and reasoning on buyer's option when stipulated description is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a stipulated desirable description is absent and apply the distinction between ikhtilaf al-naw' and ikhtilaf al-jins.

Core Idea
Missing a stipulated desirable description gives the buyer an option but does not invalidate the sale.

When a sale includes a stipulation about a desirable attribute—like a slave being a baker—and that attribute is missing, the buyer is not forced to accept a defective contract. The Hanafi school holds that the contract remains valid, but the buyer has the option to either keep the item at the full price or return it. This ruling is based on the principle that such descriptions are subordinate to the essence of the sold object, and their absence only affects the buyer's consent, not the contract's validity. The key distinction is between a mere qualitative difference (ikhtilaf al-naw') and a fundamental difference in type (ikhtilaf al-jins) which voids the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act: buying a slave with a stipulation that he is a baker or scribe, but he turns out not to be. The ruling: the contract is valid (sahih) and the buyer has the option (khiyar) to take the slave at the full price or return him.
The contract is not void or fasid.
2
Trace the reasoning (ta'lil)
The absent description is a desirable attribute (wasf muraghghab fih). Its absence compromises the buyer's consent, but the difference between a baker and non-baker is only a difference in type (ikhtilaf al-naw') because the purposes and benefits are only slightly varied. This is like the difference between male and female animals, which also does not invalidate the sale.
The contract is not invalidated because the essence of the sold object remains.
3
Apply the analogy (qiyas)
The ruling is analogized to the absence of soundness (khiyar al-ayb): if an item is sold on condition it is free from defects but is found defective, the buyer has an option. Similarly, here the buyer has an option. However, if the buyer chooses to keep the item, he must pay the full price because descriptions have no separate portion of the price; they are subordinate.
No reduction in price is allowed.
4
Note the limiting condition
If the difference between the described and actual is extreme—such as buying a slave and finding a slave-girl (ikhtilaf al-jins)—the sale becomes void (batil) because the sold object itself is absent.
This distinction is crucial for correct application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تابعة في العقد على ماعرف تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے ایک غلام اس شرط پر خریدا کہ وہ روٹی پکانے کے فن سے واقف ہو یا کتابت جانتا ہو لیکن معلوم ہوا کہ وہ نہ روٹی پکانا جانتا ہے اور نہ کتابت میں تفاوت کم ہے جیسے حیوانات میں نر اور مادہ کا اختلاف، اختلاف النوع اور اختلاف النوع کی صورت میں عقد فاسد نہیں ہوتا کیونکہ اس صورت میں مبیع یعنی یاقوت تو موجود ہے لیکن جو وصف (سرخ ہونا ) ذکر کیا تھا یہ ایسا ہے جیسا کہ کسی نے کسی چیز کو اس شرط کے ساتھ خریدا کہ وہ عیوب سے سلامت ہو لیکن وہ چیز معیب اور عیب دار نکلی تو مشتری کو اختیار ہو گا کہ وہ لے یا واپس کرے۔ ہاں اتنی بات ضرور ہے کہ اگر وصف معدوم ہونے کے باوجود مشتری نے اس کو لینے کا ارادہ کر لیا تو پورے شہن کے بدلے لے گا یمن میں سے کمی کرنے کی اجازت نہ ہو گی۔ کیونکہ سابق میں گذر چکا کہ اوصاف چونکہ عقد میں تابع ہوتے ہیں اسلئے ان کے مقابلہ میں شمن کا کوئی حصہ نہیں آتا۔ یعنی اوصاف کے کم ، زیادہ ہونے سے من کم زیادہ نہیں ہوتا۔ فوائد یہ خیال رہے کہ اگر دو چیزوں کے درمیان تفاوت فاحش کی وجہ سے اختلاف جنس ہو گیا تو مبیع معدوم ہونے کی وجہ سے بیچ باطل ہو جائے گی۔ جیسے کسی نے غلام خرید اگر وہ باندی کی تو یہ بیچ باطل ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ میچ یعنی نظام معدوم ہے اور طبیع معدوم ہونے سے بیچ باطل ہو جاتی ہے۔ تفصیل اشرف البدایہ جلد چہارم ص ۱۲۵ ۱۲۷ پر دیکھی جاسکتی ہے۔ جمیل احمد عفی عنہ
He said: Whoever sells a slave on condition that he is a baker or scribe, and he turns out contrary to that, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes he may take him for the full price, and if he wishes he may leave him. Because this is a desirable description, so it is entitled in the contract by the stipulation; then its absence necessitates the option because he was not satisfied without it. And this refers to a difference in type due to the slight variation in purposes, so the contract is not invalidated by its absence, like the description of male and female in animals, and it becomes like the absence of the description of soundness. And if he takes him, he takes him for the full price because descriptions are not apportioned any part of the price since they are subordinate in the contract, as is known. Explanation: The scenario is that if a person bought a slave on condition that he is skilled in baking or knows calligraphy, but it turns out that he knows neither baking nor calligraphy, then the buyer has the option: if he wants to take him, he may take him for the full price; if he wants to reject, he may rescind the sale. The proof is that the skill of baking and calligraphy are desirable descriptions, and stipulating a desirable description is appropriate in a sale contract. Therefore, when this desirable description is absent, the buyer has an option because he is not satisfied without it. However, the absence of a desirable description does not invalidate the contract because the difference between a baker and a non-baker, or between a scribe and a non-scribe, is a difference in type, and a difference in type exists because the purposes and benefits between them have little variation, like the difference between male and female in animals, which is a difference in type. In a difference in type, the contract is not invalid because the sold object (like a ruby) exists, but the described attribute (like being red) is absent. Similarly, being a baker or scribe is merely a description, so its absence does not invalidate the sale. However, since the buyer's consent is compromised, he must have an option. This is like when someone buys an item on condition that it is free from defects, and it turns out to be defective, then the buyer has the option to take it or return it. But if the buyer intends to take it despite the absence of the description, he takes it for the full price, and no reduction is allowed because, as established earlier, descriptions are subordinate in the contract and no part of the price is apportioned to them. Benefits: Note that if the difference between two items is extreme such that it becomes a difference in genus, then the sale becomes void because the sold object is absent. For example, if someone bought a slave that turned out to be a slave-girl, the sale is void because the sold object is absent. Details can be seen in Ashraf al-Bidaya, volume 4, pages 125-127. Jamil Ahmad, may Allah forgive him.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the buyer's option (khiyar) when a stipulated desirable description (wasf muraghghab fih) is absent in the sold item. The author, Al-Marghinani, states the ruling: if a seller sells a slave on condition that the slave is a baker or a scribe, but the slave turns out not to possess that skill, the buyer has the choice to either take the slave at the full price or to cancel the sale. The reasoning is that the description is a desirable attribute stipulated in the contract, and its absence frustrates the buyer's consent. However, the contract remains valid and is not invalidated because the difference between a baker and a non-baker, or a scribe and a non-scribe, is considered a difference in type (ikhtilaf al-naw'), not a difference in genus (ikhtilaf al-jins). This distinction is based on the relatively small variation in purposes and benefits between them. The author analogizes this to the difference between male and female animals, which also does not invalidate a sale. The passage further explains that if the buyer chooses to take the item, he must pay the full price because descriptions are subordinate to the substance and do not have a separate portion of the price. The commentary expands with examples and clarifies that if the difference is extreme—like buying a slave and finding a slave-girl—the sale is void because the sold object itself is absent. The author also notes that this ruling is similar to the option for hidden defects (khiyar al-ayb), but the key difference is that in khiyar al-ayb the contract is complete but its effect (binding ownership) is suspended, whereas here the contract is valid from the outset.

Key Terms
وصف مرغوب فيه (wasf muraghghab fih): A desirable attribute that is stipulated in the contract, giving the buyer a right of option if it is absent.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The option to rescind a contract due to a hidden defect in the sold item.
اختلاف النوع (ikhtilaf al-naw'): A difference in type, which does not invalidate a sale, as opposed to a difference in genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person buys a slave on condition he is a baker, but the slave is not a baker, what is the ruling regarding the contract according to the Hanafi school?
A) The contract is void (batil) because the condition is not met.
B) The contract is valid but the buyer has the option to take the slave at the full price or return him.
C) The contract is valid only if the buyer agrees to a price reduction.
D) The contract is fasid (invalid) because the description is essential.

Key Takeaway
The absence of a stipulated desirable description does not invalidate a sale; it grants the buyer an option to take the item at full price or return it.

Absence of Desired Attribute Does Not Invalidate Sale
Concluding discussion on sale with missing desired attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer stipulates a desired attribute that is absent, and explain the buyer's option and its limits.

Core Idea
Missing a desired attribute does not void the sale; buyer gets an option to return or accept at full price.

This passage addresses a common scenario in sales: a buyer specifies a desired attribute (e.g., being a baker, red color) but the delivered item lacks it. The Hanafi school rules that the sale remains valid because the attribute is a subsidiary detail, not an essential element. However, since the buyer's consent was conditional on that attribute, the buyer receives an option (khiyar) to either accept the item at the full agreed price or return it. No price reduction is allowed because attributes are not allocated a portion of the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is a sale contract where the buyer stipulates a specific attribute. According to the Hanafi school, such a sale is sahih (valid) even if the attribute is absent.
The validity stems from the fact that the sold item itself exists; only a subsidiary attribute is missing.
2
Distinguish between attribute and essence
The missing attribute is a difference in type (ikhtilaf al-naw'), not a difference in genus (ikhtilaf al-jins). For example, the difference between a baker and non-baker is like the difference between male and female animals – minor. If the difference were in genus, the sale would be void because the item sold would be effectively non-existent.
This distinction is crucial: ikhtilaf al-naw' preserves the contract; ikhtilaf al-jins voids it.
3
Recognize the buyer's option
Because the buyer's consent (ridā) was disturbed by the missing attribute, the buyer has an option (khiyar) to either accept the item and pay the full price or return it. This is analogous to the option for defect (khiyar al-'ayb).
The option is automatic; the buyer does not need to prove harm – the mere absence of the stipulated attribute triggers it.
4
Note the price rule when accepting
If the buyer chooses to keep the item, he must pay the full agreed price. No deduction is permitted because attributes are subsidiary in the contract; the price is not apportioned to attributes. This principle was established earlier in the text.
This contrasts with some other schools that might allow a price reduction proportionate to the missing attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ہاں وصف مرغوب فیہ کے فوت ہونے سے عقد فاسد نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ خباز اور غیر خباز کے درمیان اختلاف او کا تب اور غیر کا حسب کے درمیان اختلاف، اختلاف في النوع ہے اور اختلاف في النوع اسلئے ہے کہ ان دونوں کی اغراض اور منافع میں تفاوت کم ہے جیسے حیوانات میں نر اور مادہ کا اختلاف، اختلاف النوع اور اختلاف النوع کی صورت میں عقد فاسد نہیں ہوتا کیونکہ اس صورت میں مبیع یعنی یاقوت تو موجود ہے لیکن جو وصف (سرخ ہونا ) ذکر کیا تھا وہ معدوم ہے۔ پس اسی طرح روٹی پکانے والا ہونا اور کا تب ہونا محض ایک وصف ہے۔ لہذا اس کے معدوم ہونے سے بیع فاسد نہیں ہوگی۔ مگر چونکہ مشتری کی رضامندی مختل ہوگئی ہے اسلئے مشترکی کو اختیار ضرور ہوگا ۔ اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسا کہ کسی نے کسی چیز کو اس شرط کے ساتھ خریدا کہ وہ عیوب سے سلامت ہو لیکن وہ چیز معیب اور عیب دار نکلی تو مشتری کو اختیار ہو گا کہ وہ لے یا واپس کرے۔ ہاں اتنی بات ضرور ہے کہ اگر وصف معدوم ہونے کے باوجود مشتری نے اس کو لینے کا ارادہ کر لیا تو پورے شہن کے بدلے لے گا یمن میں سے کمی کرنے کی اجازت نہ ہو گی۔ کیونکہ سابق میں گذر چکا کہ اوصاف چونکہ عقد میں تابع ہوتے ہیں اسلئے ان کے مقابلہ میں شمن کا کوئی حصہ نہیں آتا۔ یعنی اوصاف کے کم ، زیادہ ہونے سے من کم زیادہ نہیں ہوتا۔
Yes, the absence of a desired attribute does not invalidate the contract. Because the difference between a baker and a non-baker, and between a writer and a non-writer, is a difference in type (ikhtilaf fi al-naw'), and a difference in type is because the difference in their purposes and benefits is small, like the difference between male and female in animals – difference in type. In the case of a difference in type, the contract does not become invalid because in that case the sold item, such as a ruby, exists but the attribute mentioned (being red) is absent. Thus similarly, being a baker or a writer is merely an attribute. Therefore, due to its absence, the sale does not become invalid. However, since the buyer's consent is disturbed, the buyer must have the option. And this is like when someone buys something on condition that it is free from defects, but the thing turns out to be defective; then the buyer has the option to take it or return it. Yes, it is necessary that if despite the absence of the attribute the buyer intends to take it, he will take it for the full price; he is not allowed to deduct anything from the price. Because it has passed earlier that attributes, being subsidiary in the contract, no part of the price is allocated to them; that is, the price does not increase or decrease with the increase or decrease of attributes.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the effect of the absence of a desired attribute in a sale. The author explains that if a buyer stipulates an attribute (like being a baker or red color) and that attribute is missing, the contract does not become invalid (fasid) because the difference between having and not having the attribute is a difference in type (ikhtilaf fi al-naw'), not a difference in essence. The sold item itself exists, only the attribute is absent. This is compared to the difference between male and female animals, which does not invalidate the sale. However, because the buyer's consent was conditional on that attribute, the buyer has the option (khiyar) to either accept the item at full price or return it. If he chooses to accept, he must pay the full agreed price; no reduction is allowed because attributes are subsidiary and do not have a specific portion of the price allocated to them. This is analogous to the option for defect (khiyar al-'ayb). The author distinguishes this from cases where the difference is so great that it changes the genus, in which case the sale becomes void (batil) because the sold item is effectively nonexistent.

Key Terms
وصف مرغوب فیہ (wasf marghub fihi): A desired attribute that the buyer stipulates in the contract, such as being a baker or red color.
اختیار (ikhtiyar): The buyer's option to either accept the item at full price or return it upon finding the desired attribute absent.
اختلاف النوع (ikhtilaf al-naw'): Difference in type or species, which does not invalidate the sale as opposed to difference in genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases a red ruby but it turns out to be blue, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The sale is void because the attribute is missing.
B) The sale is valid but the buyer has no option.
C) The sale is valid and the buyer can return it or accept at full price.
D) The buyer can deduct a portion of the price for the missing attribute.

Key Takeaway
Missing a desired attribute does not void the sale; the buyer gets a free option to return or keep the item at the full price.

The Option of Inspection (Khiyar al-Ru'ya): Hanafi Permissibility vs. Shafi'i Invalidity
Introduces khiyar al-ru'ya and the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya and evaluate the Shafi'i objection, applying the hadith proof.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits a sale of an unseen item with the buyer's option, based on an explicit hadith.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya), where a buyer purchases an item without seeing it first. The Hanafi school holds the sale valid but grants the buyer the option to confirm or rescind upon seeing the item. This position is contested by Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues such a sale is invalid due to uncertainty. We will explore the textual proof and the legal reasoning behind the Hanafi stance.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the sale is valid (jai'z) and the buyer has khiyar al-ru'ya upon seeing the item.
The sale is not automatically void.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the sale is invalid because the sold item is unknown (majhul).
Uncertainty in the object of sale is a general prohibition.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i rely on the principle that a sale with unknown subject matter is batil.
Gharar in the identity of the item.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The explicit hadith overrides the general principle; the ignorance from not seeing does not lead to dispute because the buyer can return it.
The hadith is specific and cannot be abandoned.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafis allow such sales with an option; Shafi'is require prior inspection for validity.
Different schools lead to different legal outcomes in real transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار الروية

ترجمہ یہ باب خیار رویت کے بیان میں ہے

تشریح خیار رویت کو خیار عیب پر اسلئے مقدم کیا ہیکہ خیار رویت، خیار عیب سے اقوئی ہے۔ کیونکہ خیار رویت بیچ کو مکمل ہونے سے روکتا ہے اور خیار عیب کی صورت میں بیع تو مکمل ہو جاتی ہے مگر حکم بیع یعنی ملکیت لازم نہیں ہوتی۔ اور یہ ظاہر ہے کہ جو چیز تمام بیچ کیلئے مانع ہو وہ اس سے اقوئی ہوتی ہے جو لزوم حکم سے مانع ہوتی ہو۔

خیار رویت کی شرعی حیثیت

و من اشترى شيئا لم يره فالبيع جائز وله الخيار اذا راه ان شاء اخذه بجميع الثمن و ان شاء رده وقال الشافعي لا يصح العقد اصلا لان المبيع مجهول ولنا قوله من اشترى شيئا لم يره فله الخيار اذا راه ولان الجهالة يعدم الرؤية لا تقضى الى المنازعة لانه لو لم يوافقه يرده فصار كجهالة الوصف في المعاين المشار اليه وكذا اذا قال رضيت ثم راه له ان يرده لان الخيار معلق بالرؤية لما روينا فلايثبت قبلها وحق الفسخ بحكم انه عقد غير لا زم لا بمقتضى الحديث ولان الرضاء بالشئى قبل العلم باوصافه لا يتحقق فلا يعتبر قوله رضيت قبل الرؤية بخلاف قوله رددت
Chapter of the Option of Inspection. Translation: This chapter is about the option of inspection. Commentary: The option of inspection has been given precedence over the option of defect because the option of inspection prevents the sale from being completed while in the case of the option of defect the sale is completed but the ruling of the sale, i.e., ownership, does not become binding. And it is obvious that something that prevents the entire sale is stronger than something that prevents the binding of the ruling. The Legal Status of the Option of Inspection: And whoever buys something he has not seen, the sale is permissible and he has the option when he sees it; if he wishes he may take it for the full price, and if he wishes he may return it. Al-Shafi'i said: The contract is not valid at all because the sold item is unknown. Our proof is the saying: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen, he has the option when he sees it.' And because the ignorance due to not seeing does not lead to dispute; because if it is not to his liking he returns it, so it becomes like ignorance of the attribute in a pointed-out item that is visually present. Similarly, if he says 'I am satisfied' and then sees it, he has the right to return it because the option is attached to seeing, as we have narrated; so it is not established before seeing. And the right of termination is by virtue that the contract is non-binding, not due to the hadith's negation. And because satisfaction with something before knowing its attributes is not realized, so his saying 'I am satisfied' before seeing is not considered, unlike his saying 'I reject'.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the chapter on the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) in the book of sales. The author first explains why this chapter is placed before the chapter on the option of defect: because the option of inspection prevents the sale from being complete (since the seller can still revoke), whereas the option of defect arises after the sale is complete but prevents it from being binding. Thus the former is stronger.

The core ruling: If someone buys an item without having seen it, the sale is valid (jai'z) according to the Hanafi school, but the buyer has the option upon seeing it. This is supported by the hadith: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen, he has the option when he sees it.' Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that such a sale is invalid because the object of sale is unknown (majhul). The Hanafi response is: The ignorance due to non-visualization does not lead to dispute because if the buyer does not like it, he returns it; it is like the ignorance of an attribute in a visually present item where the buyer sees it but may not know its hidden quality. The author also addresses the case where the buyer says 'I am satisfied' before seeing: this statement is not effective because satisfaction cannot be realized without knowledge of the attributes; thus the option remains until actual seeing. However, if the buyer says 'I reject' before seeing, that is effective because rejection is possible without full knowledge.

Key Terms
خیار رویت (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of inspection: the buyer's right to confirm or rescind a sale after seeing the item if purchased without prior inspection.
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of defect: the buyer's right to return a defective item after purchase.
مجهول (majhul): Unknown; the opponent argues that the sold item is unknown because the buyer hasn't seen it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone buys an item without seeing it, what happens when he sees it?
A) The sale becomes void automatically.
B) He has no option and must accept it.
C) He has the option to either accept it at full price or return it.
D) He can only accept it but may negotiate a lower price.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the sale of an unseen item with the buyer's option to rescind upon inspection, based on a direct hadith.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah for Seller: Imam Abu Hanifa's Initial and Final Positions
Dispute within Hanafi school on seller's khiyar al-ru'yah and its evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on seller's khiyar al-ru'yah and identify the mufta bihi position with its evidence.

Core Idea
Only the buyer has khiyar al-ru'yah; the seller's initial qiyas was overridden by explicit hadith and companion practice.

This lesson examines a rare intra-Hanafi dispute where Imam Abu Hanifa initially held one view, then retracted to the opposite. The issue is whether a seller who sells an unseen item has the option to rescind upon seeing it. The text from al-Hidayah presents both the initial qiyas-based ruling and the final position rooted in hadith and companion practice. Understanding this dispute illustrates how explicit textual evidence (nass) can override a strong analogy (qiyas jaliy) in Hanafi methodology.

Guided Path
1
State the initial ruling from al-Quduri
Identify the matn's ruling: 'Whoever sells something he has not seen, there is no option for him.' This is the final position, but the text then reveals the earlier disagreement.
2
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's initial view and its basis
He held that the seller does have khiyar al-ru'yah, analogizing (qiyas) to khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-shart. The reasoning: full consent requires knowledge of attributes, which both parties lack when the item is unseen.
The initial view is a 'qaul qadim' (earlier opinion) later abandoned.
3
Identify the evidence for the retracted (final) view
The hadith 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option when he sees it' specifically attaches the option to purchase, not sale. Also the story of Uthman and Talha where Jubayr ibn Mut'im ruled only for the buyer, in the presence of Companions.
The hadith is a primary textual evidence (dalil naqli) that overrides the qiyas.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under the initial view, both buyer and seller could rescind. Under the final view, only the buyer can. This affects transactions where the seller does not see the item (e.g., inherited property).
5
Confirm the mufta bihi position and its authority
The final view (seller no khiyar) is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, established by the retraction of Imam Abu Hanifa and acceptance by the Sahibayn and later scholars.
This is recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah as the standard ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن باع مالم يره فلا خيار له وكان ابو حنيفة يقول اولا له الخيار اعتبار ابخيار العيب وخيار الشرط وهذا لان لزوم العقد بتمام الرضاء زوالاً وثبوتا ولا يتحقق ذالك الا بالعلم باوصاف المبيع وذالك بالرؤية فلم يكن البائع راضيا بالزوال ووجه القول المرجوع اليه انه معلق بالشراء لما روينا فلا يثبت دونه و روی ان عثمان بن عفان باع ارضا بالبصرة من طلحة بن عبيد الله فقيل لطلحة انك قد غبنت فقال لی الخيار لانی اشتریت مالماره وقيل لعثمان انك قد غبنت فقال لى الخيار لانى بعث مالم اره فحكما بينها بن مطعم فقضى بالخيار لطلحة وكان ذالك بمحضر من الصحابة
He said: And whoever sells something he has not seen, there is no option for him. Abu Hanifa initially said he has the option, analogizing to the option of defect and option of condition. This is because the binding of the contract depends on complete consent in both removal and establishment, and this is not realized except with knowledge of the attributes of the sold item, and that is by seeing. So the seller was not content with its removal. And the basis of the retracted view is that it (the option) is contingent upon purchase, based on what we narrated, so it is not established without it. And it is narrated that Uthman ibn Affan sold land in Basra to Talha ibn Ubaydullah. It was said to Talha: You have been cheated. He said: I have the option because I bought what I did not see. And it was said to Uthman: You have been cheated. He said: I have the option because I sold what I did not see. So they appointed Jubayr ibn Mut'im as an arbiter between them, and he ruled that Talha has the option, and this was in the presence of the Companions.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) for the seller. The text begins by stating the ruling from al-Quduri: a seller who sells an unseen item has no khiyar. Then it mentions that Imam Abu Hanifa initially held the opposite view, giving the seller khiyar by way of qiyas (analogy) on khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) and khiyar al-shart (option of condition). The reasoning is that the contract becomes binding only with complete consent of both parties—consent to transfer ownership (by seller) and consent to accept ownership (by buyer). Complete consent requires knowledge of the item's attributes, which is achieved through seeing. Since the seller has not seen the item, he cannot be fully consenting to its removal from his ownership; hence he should have the right to rescind upon seeing it. However, Imam Abu Hanifa later retracted this view (qaul marju' ilayh) and adopted the position that the seller has no khiyar al-ru'yah. The basis for the retraction is the hadith: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option when he sees it.' The option is explicitly attached to the act of purchase (shira'), and purchase is an act of the buyer, not the seller. Therefore, the khiyar is only for the buyer. The passage then provides a supporting story: Caliph Uthman sold land to Talha without either having seen it. When told they were cheated, both claimed khiyar. They referred the matter to Jubayr ibn Mut'im, who ruled that Talha (the buyer) has the option, not Uthman (the seller). This ruling occurred in the presence of many Companions, indicating tacit approval. Thus, the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is that khiyar al-ru'yah is only for the buyer, not the seller.

Key Terms
خیار الرؤیة (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option to rescind a contract upon seeing the object of sale, if it was unseen at the time of contract.
خیار العیب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to rescind a contract due to a defect in the object of sale.
خیار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The option to rescind a contract within a stipulated period agreed upon by the parties.
قول مرجوع الیہ (qaul marju' ilayh): The final view to which Imam Abu Hanifa retracted after his initial opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the final Hanafi position, who has khiyar al-ru'yah when an unseen item is sold?
A) Both the buyer and the seller have it.
B) Only the buyer has it.
C) Only the seller has it.
D) Neither the buyer nor the seller has it.

Key Takeaway
The seller does not have khiyar al-ru'yah; only the buyer does, based on the explicit hadith and companion consensus.

Dispute on Khiyar al-Ru'ya for the Seller
Intra-Hanafi dispute on seller's khiyar al-ru'ya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the final Hanafi position on khiyar al-ru'ya for the seller and explain how the hadith and companion incident support the retracted opinion.

Core Idea
Seller has no khiyar al-ru'ya – the option belongs solely to the buyer.

When a sale is concluded without the seller having seen the item, may the seller later rescind upon inspection? Imam Abu Hanifa initially said yes by analogy, but later retracted based on a specific hadith and companion practice. This lesson traces the reasoning behind both positions and establishes the mufta bihi ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the final Hanafi position
Identify that the relied-upon view is that the seller has no khiyar al-ru'ya; the buyer alone has this option.
This is the mufta bihi and the opinion of the Sahibayn.
2
Identify the initial opinion and its proof
Note that Abu Hanifa initially held the seller has the option, using qiyas (analogy) with khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-shart.
The reasoning: full consent requires knowledge through sight, lacking which the contract is not binding.
3
Examine the retraction's primary dalil
The hadith 'Whoever buys something he did not see has the option' specifically attaches the option to purchase (shira'), which is the buyer's act.
This dalil is khas (specific) and overrides qiyas.
4
Analyse the supporting companion incident
The story of Uthman and Talha where Jubayr ibn Mut'im ruled only the buyer had the option, and no companion objected, indicating tacit consensus (ijma').
This strengthens the textual proof.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the Hanafi madhhab, a seller cannot rescind after selling an unseen item; only the buyer can. Under Shafi'i, both parties may rescind.
This practical difference matters in everyday transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع
۹۸
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد هشتم
شرط پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اولا فرماتے تھے کو اس خیار حاصل ہے اور یہ اسلئے کہ عقد کا لازم ہونا پوری رضامندی پر (موقوف) ہے زوالا بھی اور ثبوتا بھی۔ اور پوری رضامندی متحقق نہ ہوگی۔ مگر مبیع کے اوصاف معلوم ہونے سے اور مبیع کے اوصاف کا علم دیکھنے سے ہوگا اسلئے بائع مبیع زائل کرنے پر راضی نہ ہوگا۔ اور جس قول کی طرف رجوع کیا گیا ہے اس کی وجہ یہ ہے خیار رویت خرید نے پر معلق ہے اس حدیث کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے روایت کی ہے اسلئے بغیر خریدے خیار رویت ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ اور مروی ہے کہ عثمان بن عفان نے اپنی ایک زمین جو بصرہ میں واقع تھی طلحہ بن عبید اللہ کے ہاتھ فروخت کی۔ پس حضرت طلحہ سے کہا گیا کہ آپ کو خسارہ ہوا تو طلحہ نے کہا کہ مجھے اختیار ہے کیونکہ میں نے ایسی چیز خریدی جس کو میں نے نہیں دیکھا اور حضرت عثمان سے کہا گیا کہ آپ کو نقصان ہوا تو فرمایا کہ مجھے خیار حاصل ہے کیونکہ میں نے ایسی چیز فروخت کی جس کو میں نے نہیں دیکھا ہے۔ پس دونوں نے جبیر بن مطعم کو اپنے درمیان حکم مقرر کیا تو جبیر بن نے طلحہ کیلئے خیار کا فیصلہ کیا اور یہ فیصلہ صحابہ کی موجودگی میں تھا۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے بغیر دیکھے کوئی چیز فروخت کی بایں طور کہ کسی چیز کا وارث ہوا اور دیکھنے سے پہلے اس کو بیچ دیا تو یہ بیچ درست ہے۔ مگر ہمارے نزدیک بائع کیلئے خیار رویت ثابت نہ ہو گا۔ حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کے قول اول کے مطابق بائع کو خیار حاصل ہے لیکن بعد میں اس قول سے رجوع کر کے عدم خیار کے قائل ہو گئے تھے۔ قول اول کی دلیل خیار عیب اور خیار شرط پر قیاس ہے۔ یعنی جس طرح بائع کو خیار عیب اور خیار شرط حاصل ہے۔ اسی طرح خیار رویت بھی حاصل ہوگا۔ خیار عیب اس طور پر حاصل ہے کہ بائع نے اگرشن کو خراب پایا تو بائع کو اختیار ہے کہ وہ اس شمن کے عوض بیچ کو جائز کر دے یا اس کو واپس کر دے۔ جیسے مشتری اگر مبیع کو عیب دار پائے تو اس کو اختیار ہوتا ہے۔ البتہ اتنا فرق ضرور ہے کہ بائع کے ثمن واپس کرنے سے عقد بیع مسیح نہیں ہوتا۔ اور مشترکی کا مبیع واپس کرنے سے عقد بیع فسخ ہو جاتا ہے۔ کیونکہ عقد بیع میں مبیع اصل ہوتی ہے ثمن اصل نہیں ہوتا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ بائع کیلئے خنیار رویت کا ثابت ہونا اسلئے بھی ہے کہ عقد بیع اس وقت لازم ہوگا جبکہ بائع اپنی ملک فائل کرنے پر اور مشتری اپنے لئے ملک ثابت کرنے پر راضی ہو اور یہ رضامندی اس ہوسکتا۔ پس بائع نے چونکہ مبیع کو نہیں دیکھا ہے۔ اسلئے اس کو مبیع کے اوصاف کا علم بھی حاصل نہ ہوگا اور جب بائع کو مبیع کے اوصاف کا علم حاصل نہیں ہوا تو بائع مبیع سے اپنی ملک زائل کرنے پر بھی پورے طور سے راضی نہ ہوگا اور جب بائع کی رضا مندی نہیں پائی گئی تو عقد بھی لازم نہ ہوگا اور عقد لازم نہ ہونے کی صورت میں فتح کا اختیار ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے بائع کو بغیر دیکھے فروخت کرنے کی صورت میں بیع فتح کر دینے کا اختیار ہے۔
امام ابو حنیفہ نے جس قول کی طرف رجوع کیا ہے اس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ "خیار رویت " حديث من اشترى شيئاً لـم يـرة فـلـه الخیار کی وجہ سے شراء پر متعلق ہے۔ اس لئے بغیر شراء کے خیار رویت ثابت نہ ہوگا اور شراء ( خریدنا) چونکہ مشتری کی جانب سے پایا جاتا ہے نہ کہ بائع کی جانب سے اسلئے خیار رویت مشتری ہی کیلئے ثابت ہوگا نہ کہ بائع کیلئے۔
قول مرجوع الیہ کی تائید اس واقعہ سے بھی ہوتی ہے جس کو امام طحاوی اور امام بیہقی نے علقمہ بن ابی وقاص سے روایت کیا ہے۔ واقعہ یہ ہے کہ حضرت عثمان بن عفان نے اپنی ایک زمین جو بصرہ میں واقع تھی ۔ حضرت طلحہ بن عبید اللہ کے ہاتھ فروخت کی۔ حضرت طلحہ سے کہا گیا کہ آپ کو خسارہ ہو گیا یہ سن کر حضرت طلحہ نے فرمایا کہ مجھے اختیار حاصل ہے۔ کیونکہ میں نے بغیر دیکھے زمین کو خریدا ہے۔ اور حضرت
عثمان سے کہا گیا کہ آپ کو نقصان ہوا ہے انہوں نے فرمایا کہ مجھے اختیار حاصل ہے کیونکہ میں نے اپنی زمین بغیر دیکھے فروخت کی ہے۔ پس دونوں حضرات نے جبیر بن مطعم کو حکم مقرر کر لیا تو حضرت جبیر بن مطعم نے فیصلہ کیا کہ حضرت طلحہ کو خیار حاصل ہے۔ اور یہ واقعہ صحابہ کی موجودگی میں پیش آیا مگر کسی نے نکیر نہیں فرمائی۔ تو گویا صحابہ کا اس بات پر اجماع ہو گیا کہ خیار رویت مشتری کو حاصل ہوگا نہ کہ بائع کو۔
Book of Sales. ... First he said that he has this option based on analogy with condition, and that is because the contract's binding depends on full consent, both in removal and in establishment. And full consent will not be realized except by knowing the attributes of the sold item, and knowledge of the attributes of the sold item comes from seeing. Therefore the seller will not consent to remove his ownership. And the reason for the opinion he retracted to is that the option of inspection is attached to purchase due to the hadith we narrated, so without purchase the option is not established. And it is narrated that Uthman ibn Affan sold a land of his in Basra to Talha ibn Ubaydullah. Then Talha was told, 'You have incurred a loss.' Talha said, 'I have the choice because I bought something I did not see.' And Uthman was told, 'You have suffered a loss.' He said, 'I have the option because I sold something I did not see.' Then both appointed Jubayr ibn Mut'im as arbiter, and Jubayr ruled for Talha having the option, and this ruling was in the presence of the Companions. Explanation: The issue is that if someone sells something without seeing it, e.g., he inherited something and sold it before seeing, the sale is valid, but in our view the seller does not have the option of inspection. According to Imam Abu Hanifa's first opinion, the seller has the option, but later he retracted and held no option. The proof of the first opinion is analogy on option of defect and option of condition. Just as the seller has option of defect and option of condition, similarly he would have option of inspection. The option of defect is such that if the seller finds the price defective, he has the choice to validate the sale for that price or return it, just as the buyer if he finds the sold item defective has choice. But there is a difference: the seller's returning the price does not dissolve the sale, while the buyer's returning the sold item dissolves the sale because in a sale the sold item is the principal, not the price. The author of al-Hidayah says that the option of inspection is established for the seller also because the sale is binding only when the seller is satisfied to transfer ownership and the buyer is satisfied to acquire ownership, and this satisfaction is realized only when both parties know all attributes of the sold item, and knowledge of attributes comes from seeing because details learned by seeing cannot be known by description. Since the seller did not see the sold item, he does not have knowledge of its attributes, and thus he is not fully satisfied to transfer ownership, so the contract is not binding, and when the contract is not binding, he has the option to rescind. Therefore, when selling without seeing, the seller has the option to rescind. The proof for the opinion to which Imam Abu Hanifa retracted is that 'option of inspection' is attached to purchase due to the hadith 'Whoever buys something he did not see has the option.' Therefore, without purchase, the option is not established, and purchase is from the buyer, not the seller, so the option of inspection is for the buyer only, not the seller. This retracted opinion is supported by the incident narrated by Imam al-Tahawi and Imam al-Bayhaqi from Alqamah ibn Abi Waqqas: Uthman ibn Affan sold his land in Basra to Talha ibn Ubaydullah. Talha was told, 'You incurred a loss.' He said, 'I have the option because I bought land without seeing it.' Uthman was told, 'You incurred a loss.' He said, 'I have the option because I sold my land without seeing it.' Then both appointed Jubayr ibn Mut'im as arbiter, and Jubayr ruled that Talha has the option. This incident occurred in the presence of the Companions and none objected, so it is as if there was consensus of the Companions that the option of inspection belongs to the buyer, not the seller.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
Passage type: Intra-Hanafi dispute. The text discusses the ruling on whether the seller has khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) in a sale where the seller sold an item without having seen it. Initially, Imam Abu Hanifa held that the seller has this option by analogy (qiyas) with khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) and khiyar al-shart (option of condition). The reasoning is that full consent of both parties requires knowledge of the object's attributes, which is obtained through sight; without sight, the seller's consent is incomplete, so the contract is not binding and the seller may rescind. However, Abu Hanifa later retracted this opinion and adopted the view that the seller does not have khiyar al-ru'ya. The basis for the retraction is the hadith 'Whoever buys something he did not see has the option,' which specifically attaches the option to the purchaser. Since the buyer is the one who purchases, the option belongs only to him. This view is further supported by the well-known incident of Uthman and Talha, where the arbiter Jubayr ibn Mut'im ruled that Talha (the buyer) had the option, and no Companion objected, indicating tacit consensus (ijma'). The final Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is that khiyar al-ru'ya is established only for the buyer, not the seller. The text also notes that if someone sells inherited property without seeing it, the sale is valid but the seller has no khiyar.

Key Terms
خیار الرؤیت (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option to rescind a sale after inspecting the sold item, given to a party who concluded the contract without having seen the item.
قول اول (qawl awwal): Imam Abu Hanifa's initial opinion, before retraction, that the seller also has khiyar al-ru'ya.
قول مرجوع الیہ (qawl marju' ilayh): The opinion to which Imam Abu Hanifa retracted, i.e., that the seller does not have khiyar al-ru'ya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the final Hanafi position (mufta bihi), who has khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) when an item is sold without being seen?
A) Only the seller
B) Both the buyer and the seller
C) Only the buyer
D) Neither party

Key Takeaway
The seller has no khiyar al-ru'ya in Hanafi fiqh; the option belongs exclusively to the buyer.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Duration and Nullifiers of Khiyar al-Ru'ya
Ruling statement with expansion on nullifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the actions that nullify khiyar al-ru'ya and explain why the option is not time-bound in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'ya lasts until a nullifier occurs; disposals that create irrevocable or third-party rights nullify it immediately.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) as presented in al-Hidayah. The passage establishes that this option is not bound by time but remains until a specific nullifier (mubtil) occurs. It then provides a detailed categorization of disposals (tasarrufat) that nullify the option, distinguishing between those that do so before and after inspection. Understanding this classification is essential for applying the option correctly in sale transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
The text opens by stating that khiyar al-ru'ya is not time-bound (ghayr muwaqqat). This is the core ruling: the buyer's option to rescind after seeing the item persists until a nullifying event occurs, not until a fixed period expires.
Some Hanafi mashayikh disagreed and held it is time-limited after inspection, but the preferred (mukhtar) view is no time limit.
2
Understand the analogy to khiyar al-shart
The nullifiers for khiyar al-ru'ya are the same as those for khiyar al-shart: a defect in the sold item (ta'ayyub) or a disposal (tasarruf) that indicates consent. However, the text clarifies that not every disposal nullifies; there is a detailed classification.
Sahib al-Quduri stated the analogy broadly; al-Hidayah provides the refinement.
3
Classify nullifying disposals
Three categories of disposals are identified: (1) irrevocable disposals (e.g., emancipation, tadbir) nullify before and after inspection; (2) disposals creating a third-party right (e.g., absolute sale, pledge, lease) also nullify before and after; (3) disposals not creating a third-party right (e.g., sale with option, bargaining, gift without delivery) nullify only after inspection, not before.
The key distinction is whether the disposal is binding (lazim) or creates a right for another. If so, rescission is impossible, so the option is void immediately.
4
Apply the rule to before and after inspection
For disposals in categories 1 and 2, the option is nullified even before the buyer sees the item because the disposal makes the contract binding. For category 3, the disposal before inspection is merely an expression of interest and does not indicate definitive consent, so the option remains. After inspection, however, such disposals imply consent and thus nullify the option.
This distinction protects the buyer's right to rescind before inspection when actions are not final.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some earlier Hanafi scholars held that after inspection, the option is time-limited to the period in which rescission is possible (e.g., before taking delivery). The text rejects this and affirms the mukhtar view: no time limit, only nullifiers.
The tarjih authority is al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah, and this is the mufta bihi position.
