ترجمہ پھر خیار رویت وقت کے ساتھ متعین نہیں ہے بلکہ باقی رہے گا یہاں تک کہ کوئی ایسا امر پایا جائے جو اس کو باطل کر دے۔ اور جو عیب اور تصرف خیار شرط کو باطل کرتا ہے وہ خیار رویت کو بھی باطل کرتا ہے۔ پھر اگر ایسا تصرف ہو جس کا ختم کرنا ممکن نہیں ہے جیسے آزاد کرنا اور مدبر کرنا یا ایسا تصرف جو دوسرے کا حق واجب کرتا ہے جیسے بیع مطلق اور رہن اور اجارہ تو یہ خیار رویت کو باطل کر دے گا رویت سے پہلے بھی اور رویت کے بعد بھی۔ کیونکہ تصرف جب لا زم ہو گیا تو ضیح کرنا متعذر ہو گیا اور خیار باطل ہو گیا اور اگر ایسا تصرف ہو جو غیر کا حق واجب نہیں کرتا جیسے شرط خیار کے ساتھ بیچنا، بھاؤ کرنا اور بغیر قبضہ دلائے ہبہ کرنا تو یہ دیکھنے سے پہلے خیار رویت کو باطل نہیں کرتا۔ کیونکہ یہ تصرف صریح رضامندی سے بڑھ کر نہیں ہے اور دیکھنے کے بعد یہ تصرف خیار رویت باطل کرتا ہے کیونکہ دلالۃ رضامندی پائی گئی ہے۔
تشریح ہمارے نزدیک خیار رویت کسی وقت کے ساتھ محدود نہیں ہے بلکہ اس وقت تک باقی رہے گا جب تک کہ کوئی ایسا امر نہ پایا جائے جو اس کو باطل کر دے۔ اگر چہ بعض مشائخ کا مذہب یہ ہے کہ خیار رویت، رویت طبیع کے بعد وقت امکان فسخ کے ساتھ موقت اور معین ہے۔ یعنی اگر مشتری رویت مبیع کے بعد بیع نسخ کرنے پر قادر ہو گیا مگر فسخ نہیں کی تو اسکا خیار رویت باطل اور عقد بیع لازم ہو گیا۔ بہر حال مذہب مختار یہی ہے کہ خیار رویت کسی وقت کے ساتھ محدود اور موقت نہیں ہے بلکہ مبطل خیار کے پائے جانے تک باقی رہے گا۔ رہی یہ بات کہ مبطل (باطل کرنے والی) کیا چیز ہے تو اس بارے میں صاحب قدوری نے فرمایا کہ جو چیز خیار شرط کو باطل کرتی ہے وہ خیار رویت کو بھی باطل کر دے گی۔ مثلاً مبیع کا عیب دار ہو جانا اور مبیع کے اندر تصرف کرنا۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مبیع کے اندر مطلق تصرف خیار رویت کو باطل نہیں کرتا بلکہ اس میں قدرے تفصیل ہے۔ چنانچہ مشتری نے اگر مبیع کے اندر ایسا تصرف کر ڈالا جس کو فسخ کرنا اور ختم کرنا ممکن نہیں رہا۔ مثلاً بغیر دیکھے غلام خریدا اور پھر اس کو آزاد کر دیا یا اس کو مد بر کر دیا۔ یا مشتری نے ایسا تصرف کیا جو دوسرے کا حق واجب کرتا ہے۔ مثلاً ایک چیز بغیر دیکھے خریدی پھر اس کو بغیر خیار شرط کے فروخت کر دیا۔ یا رویت سے پہلے ہی اس کو کسی کے پاس رہن رکھ دیا یا کسی کو اجرت پر دے دیا تو یہ دونوں قسم کے تصرف رویت مبیع سے پہلے بھی خیار
Then, the option of inspection is not time-bound, but remains until something occurs that nullifies it. And what nullifies the option of condition—a defect or a disposal—nullifies the option of inspection. Then, if the disposal is one that cannot be rescinded, like emancipation or tadbir, or a disposal that creates a right for another, like an absolute sale, pledge, or lease, it nullifies [the option] both before and after inspection because when the disposal becomes binding, rescission becomes impossible, so the option is nullified. And if the disposal is one that does not create a right for another, like a sale with an option, bargaining, or a gift without delivery, it does not nullify before inspection because it does not exceed explicit consent, but it nullifies after inspection because there is evidence of consent. Translation: Then, the option of inspection is not fixed with time, but remains until something is found that nullifies it. And whatever defect or disposal nullifies the option of condition also nullifies the option of inspection. Then, if the disposal is one that cannot be ended, like emancipation or tadbir, or a disposal that creates a right for another, like an absolute sale, pledge, or lease, it will nullify the option of inspection both before and after inspection. Because when the disposal becomes binding, rescission becomes impossible, and the option is nullified. And if the disposal is one that does not create a right for another, like selling with an option, bargaining, or gifting without delivery, it does not nullify the option of inspection before seeing because this disposal does not exceed explicit consent, and after seeing it nullifies the option because evidence of consent is found. Explanation: In our view, the option of inspection is not limited by time, but remains until something is found that nullifies it. Although some mashayikh hold that the option of inspection, after natural inspection, is time-limited with the period of possible rescission—i.e., if the buyer becomes able to rescind after seeing the sold item but does not rescind, his option of inspection is nullified and the contract becomes binding. Nevertheless, the preferred madhhab is that the option of inspection is not limited or fixed with time, but remains until a nullifier is found. As for what the nullifier is, Sahib al-Quduri said that whatever nullifies the option of condition also nullifies the option of inspection, e.g., the sold item becoming defective or a disposal in the sold item. Sahib al-Hidayah says that an absolute disposal in the sold item does not nullify the option of inspection; rather there is detail. So, if the buyer makes a disposal that cannot be rescinded, e.g., buys a slave without seeing then emancipates him or makes him mudabbar, or the buyer makes a disposal that creates a right for another, e.g., buys something without seeing then sells it absolutely without an option, or before inspection pledges it or leases it—both types of disposal nullify the option even before inspection.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
Passage type: Ruling statement (with expansion). The text addresses the duration of khiyar al-ru'ya and what actions nullify it. The key ruling is that khiyar al-ru'ya is not time-bound (ghayr muwaqqat); it continues until a nullifying cause (mubtil) occurs. The text then classifies nullifying disposals (tasarrufat) into three categories: (1) Disposals that are irrevocable, like emancipation ('itq) and tadbir (declaring a slave free after death) – these nullify the option both before and after inspection. (2) Disposals that create a third-party right, like an absolute sale, pledge (rahn), or lease (ijara) – these also nullify the option both before and after inspection because they make rescission impossible. (3) Disposals that do not create a third-party right, like selling with an option (khiyar al-shart), bargaining (musawama), or gift without delivery (hiba ghayr muqabbada) – these do not nullify the option before inspection because they do not indicate definitive consent, but they do nullify the option after inspection because the buyer's action after seeing the item implies consent. The text also notes a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars: some earlier mashayikh held that khiyar al-ru'ya is time-limited after inspection, but the preferred (mukhtar) view is that it is not limited by time. The nullifier is defined analogously to khiyar al-shart, but with the detailed classification provided by al-Hidayah. The passage appears to be cut off; the original likely continues with more examples or the conclusion of the discussion.

Key Terms
خیار الرؤیت (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option to rescind a sale after inspecting the sold item, given to the buyer who did not see it before the contract.
موقت (muwaqqat): Time-bound; the text states khiyar al-ru'ya is not muwaqqat.
مبطل (mubtil): That which nullifies the option, such as a defect or a specific type of disposal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Bargaining (musawama) for a lower price
B) Selling the item unconditionally to a third party
C) Gifting the item without delivery
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'ya is not time-bound; it remains until a nullifying disposal occurs, with disposals that are irrevocable or create third-party rights nullifying it even before inspection.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah: No Fixed Time Limit and Actions That Void It
Explains khiyar al-ru'yah's non-time-bound nature and voiding actions with proof from Companions' consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration of khiyar al-ru'yah and identify which dispositions void it before and after sight.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'yah is not time-bound; it remains until a voiding action occurs.

In this lesson, we examine the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) as established by the Companions' practice. The classical Hanafi texts clarify that this option does not expire after a fixed period, unlike contractual options. Instead, it persists until the buyer performs an action that implicitly or explicitly indicates acceptance or until a defect arises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on duration
The passage states that khiyar al-ru'yah is non-time-bound (ghayr muwaqqat). It remains until something that voids it occurs.
This is the mufta bihi position, though some early shuyukh held it is time-bound.
2
Recognize the proof: Ijma' of the Companions
The story of Talhah and Jubayr ibn Mut'im shows that the Companions did not object to granting khiyar al-ru'yah to the buyer. Their silence (sukut) constitutes tacit ijma' (ijma' sukuti) that the option belongs to the buyer.
Hanafi usul accepts ijma' sukuti as a valid proof.
3
List the voiding factors
The voiding factors are the same as for khiyar al-shart: a defect (ayb) in the object or a disposition (tasarruf) by the buyer.
This analogy between khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-shart is from al-Quduri.
4
Distinguish two categories of dispositions
Dispositions that are irrevocable or create third-party rights (e.g., emancipation, absolute sale, pledge, lease) void the option before and after sight. Dispositions that do not create third-party rights (e.g., sale with option, negotiation, gift without delivery) void only after sight, as they then indicate implicit consent.
Before sight, even explicit consent does not void the option; after sight, implicit consent suffices.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some early Hanafi shuyukh held that khiyar al-ru'yah is time-bound to the period after sight during which rescission is possible. The mukhtar position is non-time-bound.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah prefers the non-time-bound view, and this is the mufta bihi.

Then the option of sight is not time-bound; rather it remains until something that voids it occurs. And whatever voids the option of condition, such as a defect or a disposition, voids the option of sight. Then if the disposition is one that cannot be undone, like emancipation or tadbir, or a disposition that creates a right for others, like an absolute sale, pledge, or lease, it voids it before sight and after, because when the disposition becomes binding, rescission becomes impossible, so the option is void. And if the disposition does not create a right for others, like a sale with the option of condition, negotiation, or gift without delivery, it does not void it before sight because it is not explicit consent, but it voids it after sight due to the presence of implicit consent.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Quduri's matn with commentary from al-Hidayah. It establishes that khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) is not limited to a specific time period; it remains valid until something occurs that explicitly voids it. The commentator explains that the voiding factors are the same as for khiyar al-shart (option of condition), namely the occurrence of a defect in the object or a disposition (tasarruf) by the buyer. The text then distinguishes between two categories of disposition: those that cannot be undone (like freeing a slave or making a mudabbar) or that create rights for third parties (like absolute sale, pledge, or lease) — these void the option both before and after sight. Dispositions that do not create third-party rights (like selling with an option, bargaining, or gifting without delivery) do not void the option before sight because they are not explicit consent, but they do void it after sight because they indicate consent implicitly. The commentary also notes an internal Hanafi disagreement: some early scholars held that the option is time-bound to the period after sight during which rescission is possible, but the relied-upon (mukhtar) position is that it remains until a voiding factor arises. The text concludes by citing a demonstration from the practice of the Companions (ijma') showing that khiyar al-ru'yah belongs to the buyer, not the seller.

Key Terms
خیار رؤیت (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of sight: the buyer's right to rescind a sale after seeing the item if it does not meet expectations, having purchased it unseen.
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): The option of condition: a contractual option agreed by the parties, typically for a specified period.
اجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the Companions, inferred from their silence (sukut) in the presence of a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The buyer sells the item with an option condition to a third party.

D) The buyer gifts the item without delivering possession.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'yah remains valid indefinitely until a voiding action occurs; dispositions that are irrevocable or create third-party rights void it even before sight, while others void it only after sight.

Sufficient Sight to Remove Khiyar al-Ru'yah
Ruling on what sight suffices for khiyar al-ru'yah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criteria for sufficient sight in khiyar al-ru'yah and apply them to different types of sold goods.

Core Idea
Seeing the entire item is not required; sight that reveals the intended purpose is sufficient.

In this passage from al-Quduri, we encounter the core Hanafi principle for khiyar al-ru'yah: the buyer need not see every inch of the item. Instead, seeing what indicates the purpose—like the face of a slave-girl or the surface of a grain pile—removes the option. This lesson walks through the legal reasoning and the key distinctions for homogenous vs. variegated goods, animals, and edibles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
The text states that seeing the entirety is not required due to impracticability. Instead, sight that indicates knowledge of the intended purpose is sufficient. Establish this as the foundation.
The 'intended purpose' varies by item type.
2
Classify the Goods
Distinguish between homogenous items (mukayyal/mawzun) where individuals do not vary, and variegated items (like garments, animals) that do vary. For homogenous, a sample or surface view suffices; for variegated, each must be seen unless the item's intended part is uniform.
Examples: grain vs. bundles of cloth.
3
Apply to Specific Cases
Work through the examples: surface of a pile, outer layer of a folded garment, face of a human, face and hindquarters of an animal. Note that for items with a specific intended part, only that part need be seen.
For breeding animals, the udder is intended; for meat, the belly.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Identify the difference: some required seeing the legs of an animal; the relied-upon view from Abu Yusuf requires only face and hindquarters. Understand that the chosen view is recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The legs are not part of the intended purpose for most buyers.
5
Extend to Edibles and Special Cases
For meat sheep: belly must be seen. For breeding sheep: udder must be seen. For edible items: tasting is required. This shows the rule adapts to the purpose.
Al-Karkhi considered walnuts and eggs like measured items.

Source Text

قال و من نظر الى وجه الصبرة او الى ظاهر الثوب مطويا او الى وجه الجارية او الى وجه الدابة وكفلها فلا خيار له والاصل فى هذا ان رؤية جميع المبيع غير مشروط لتعذره فيكتفى بروية ما يدل على العلم بالمقصود ولو دخل فى البيع اشياء فان كان لايتفاوت احادها كالمكيل والموزون وعلامته ان يعرض بالنموذج يكتفى برؤية واحد منها الا اذا كان الباقي اردأ مما رأى فحينذ يكون له الخيار ان كان يتفاوت احـادهـا كالثياب والدواب لابد من رؤية كل واحد منها والجوزو البيض من هذا القبيل فيما ذكره الكرخي وكان ينبغي ان يكون مثل الحنطة والشعير لكونها متقاربة اذاثبت هذا فنقول النظر الى وجه الصيرة كاف لانه يعرف وصف البقية لانه مكيل يعرض بالنموذج وكذا النظر الى ظاهر الثوب مما يعلم البقية الا اذا كان في طيه مايكون مقصودا كموضع العلم والوجه هو المقصود في الادمى وهو و الكفل فى الدواب فيعتبر رؤية المقصود ولا يعتبر رؤية غيره وشرط بعضهم رؤية القوائم والاول هو المروى عن ابي يوسف وفي شاة اللحم لابد من الحب لان المقصود وهو اللحم يعرف به وفى شاة القنيه لابد من رؤية الضرع وفيما يطعم لا بد من الذوق لان ذالك هو المعرف للمقصود
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا اور جس شخص نے ڈھیر کے اوپر کو دیکھ لیایاتہ کئے ہوئے تھان کے اوپر کے حصہ کو دیکھ لیا۔ یا باندی کے چہرے یا چوپایہ کے چہرے اور سرین کو دیکھ لیا تو اس کیلئے خیار رویت نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اس باب میں اصل یہ ہے کہ تمام بیع کا دیکھنا مشروط نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ متعذر ہے۔ پس اتنی بیع کا دیکھنا کافی ہو گا جو مقصود سے واقف ہونے پر دلالت کرے۔ اور اگر بیع میں ایک ہی جنس کی چند چیزیں داخل ہوں پس اگر ان کے افراد متفاوت نہ ہوں جیسے کیلی اور وزنی چیزیں اور اس کی علامت یہ ہے کہ نمونہ کے طور پر پیش کی
He said: And whoever looks at the face of a pile, or at the surface of a folded garment, or at the face of a slave-girl, or at the face and hindquarters of an animal, then he has no option. The principle in this is that seeing the entirety of the sold item is not required due to its impracticability, so seeing what indicates knowledge of the intended purpose is sufficient. And if items enter the sale, then if they are not variegated (individuals are similar), like measured and weighed items – their indicator being presented by sample – it is sufficient to see one of them, unless the rest are worse than what he saw, then he has the option. If they are variegated, like garments and animals, it is necessary to see each one. Walnuts and eggs are of this category according to what al-Karkhi mentioned. And it should be that they are like wheat and barley because they are similar. Now if this is established, we say: looking at the face of the pile is sufficient because the description of the rest is known, as it is measured and presented by sample. Similarly, looking at the surface of the garment makes known the rest, unless there is something in its fold that is intended, like the place of embroidery. The face is the intended part in humans, and the face and hindquarters in animals; so seeing the intended part is considered, and seeing other parts is not considered. Some scholars required seeing the legs, but the first is narrated from Abu Yusuf. In a sheep for meat, it is necessary to see the belly because the intended (meat) is known thereby. In a breeding sheep, it is necessary to see the udder. In edible items, tasting is necessary because that is what makes the intended known.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Quduri on what constitutes sufficient sight to eliminate khiyar al-ru'yah. It establishes that the buyer does not need to see every part of the item; it is enough to see what reveals the essential characteristics. For a heap of grain, looking at the surface suffices because grain is homogenous. For a folded garment, looking at the outer layer suffices unless there is a specific feature like embroidery hiding in the folds. For a slave-girl, looking at the face is enough; for an animal, looking at the face and hindquarters is enough. When multiple items are sold together, if they are of a type that does not vary individually (like grain, oil), seeing one is enough unless the remainder is inferior. If they vary (like garments, animals), each must be seen. There is a difference within the Hanafi school: some require seeing the legs of an animal, but the relied-upon report from Abu Yusuf is that only the face and hindquarters are needed. For a sheep intended for meat, seeing the belly is required to judge the meat; for a breeding sheep, seeing the udder is required. For edible items, tasting is required. The underlying principle is that sight must convey knowledge of the intended benefit.

Key Terms
صبرة (sibrah): A heap or pile of grain or similar item; looking at its face (surface) is sufficient for khiyar al-ru'yah.
تمثیل / نموذج (tamthil / namudhaj): Sample or representative specimen; sufficient for items that are uniform.
شاة لحم / شاة قنیة (shaat lahm / shaat qinyah): Sheep for meat vs. breeding sheep; different body parts are inspected for each purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if someone buys a pile of wheat and looks only at the surface, does he have khiyar al-ru'yah?
A) Yes, because he must see the entire pile.
B) No, because the surface suffices for measured items.
C) Yes, unless he saw a sample.
D) No, only if he tastes it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle for khiyar al-ru'yah: sight of what indicates the intended purpose, not the entire item, removes the option.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Effect of Buyer's Disposition on Khiyar al-Ru'yah
Explains when buyer's actions void khiyar al-ru'yah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish dispositions that void khiyar al-ru'yah from those that do not, and apply the rule that implied consent before inspection does not void the option.

Core Idea
Dispositions making rescission impossible or creating third-party rights void khiyar; implied consent before inspection does not.

When a buyer sees an item and is dissatisfied, he may rescind the sale due to khiyar al-ru'yah. But what if the buyer acts on the item before inspection? This lesson clarifies which actions (tasarrufat) nullify the option and which do not, based on the Hanafi principle of implied consent (dalalat al-rida).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disposition
Read the buyer's action described in the passage (e.g., selling with a condition, gifting without possession). Determine whether it is a disposition (tasarruf).
A disposition is any act that indicates ownership or intent regarding the sold item.
2
Assess effect: impossible rescission or third-party right?
If the disposition makes rescission impossible (e.g., selling to a third party with full transfer) or creates a right for another (e.g., giving possession), then the option is void even before inspection.
If no such effect, the option remains.
3
Determine timing: before or after inspection
If the disposition occurs before inspection and does not create a third-party right, it is mere implied consent and does not void the option (by analogy to explicit consent before inspection not voiding it). After inspection, any implied consent voids the option.
Before inspection, explicit consent does not void; so implied consent a fortiori does not.
4
Apply to scenario
Given a concrete scenario (e.g., buyer sells the item before seeing it with a three-day option), state whether khiyar remains or is voided.

مبیع کا دیکھنا نا ممکن ہوتا ہے جیسا کہ مبیع اگر غلام یا باندی ہو تو ان کے پورے بدن کو دیکھنے کیلئے ان کی شرمگاہوں اور ان کے ستر عورت کا دیکھنا بھی ضروری ہوگا حالانکہ یہ شرعاً حرام ہے۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ پوری صبیح کا دیکھنا متعذر ہے۔ اسی طرح تہہ کئے ہوئے اور لیٹے ہوئے تمان کو کھولنے سے بائع کو ضرر ہوگا کہ پورے تھان کی تہہ ٹوٹ کر تھان ہی خراب ہو جائے گا۔ اسی طرح ڈھیر کے ہر ہر دانہ کو دیکھنا مستعد رہے۔ پس جب پوری طبیع کا دیکھنا متعذر ہے تو طبی میں تنی چیز کا دیکھنا کافی ہوگا جس سے مقصود کا علم حاصل ہو جائے اور اگر مبیع دوسری قسم یعنی اشیاء متعدده متقارب الآحاد ہیں جیسے ملکیلی اور موزونی چیزیں اور عددی متقارب، اور اخروٹ اور مرغی کے انڈے مصنف کے بیان کے مطابق
They invalidate the option of inspection even after seeing. The proof is that when the disposition made by the buyer becomes impossible to rescind, or when the disposition makes another's right obligatory, then rescinding the sale becomes impossible. When rescinding the sale becomes impossible, the buyer's option of inspection also becomes void. And if the buyer made a disposition in the sale that does not establish another's right — for example, he bought something without seeing, then sold it with the condition of a three-day option, or gave it for appraisal without seeing, or gifted it but did not give possession to the donee — in these three cases, a disposition occurred from the buyer, but it was not a disposition that makes another's right obligatory. Therefore, such a disposition before inspection does not void the buyer's option of inspection, because this disposition is an implied indication of consent. And it has already passed that before seeing the commodity, explicit expression of consent does not void the option of inspection. So when explicit consent before inspection does not void the option, implied consent a fortiori does not void it. Yes, after inspection, this type of disposition will void the option of inspection, because in that case implied consent is found, and just as after seeing the commodity explicit consent drops the option, so too implied consent drops it. He looked at the surface of a heap of wheat, or at the outer part of a folded cloth, looked at the face of a slave-girl, and looked at the face and hindquarters of an animal — for him there is no option. He said: Whoever looks at the surface of a heap, or at the outer part of a cloth folded, or at the face of a slave-girl, or at the face and hindquarters of an animal, then there is no option for him. The principle in this matter is that seeing the entire sold item is not required because it is impossible; so seeing that which indicates knowledge of the intended purpose suffices. And if things are included in the sale, then if their units do not differ — like measured and weighed items, and their sign is that they are presented as a sample — seeing one of them suffices, unless the rest are inferior, in which case he has the option. If their units differ — like cloths and animals — seeing each one is necessary. Nuts and eggs are of this category according to what al-Karkhi mentioned, but it would be appropriate that they be like wheat and barley because they are similar. Once this is established, we say: looking at the face of the heap suffices, because the description of the rest is known from it, since it is a measured item presented as a sample. Similarly, looking at the outer part of the cloth is of this category, because the rest is known from it, unless inside it there is something intended, like the place of embroidery. The face is what is intended in a human, and the face and hindquarters in animals. So seeing the intended part is considered, and seeing other than it is not considered. Some have stipulated seeing the limbs, but the first is narrated from Abu Yusuf. In a sheep for meat, weighing is necessary because the intended meat is known by it. In a sheep for keeping, seeing the udder is necessary. In edible things, tasting is necessary because that is what gives knowledge of the intended purpose. Translation: Al-Quduri said: Whoever looks at the top of a heap, or at the upper part of a folded cloth, or at the face of a slave-girl, or at the face and hindquarters of an animal, then there is no option of inspection for him. The principle in this chapter is that seeing all of the sold item is not required because it is impossible. So seeing that much which indicates acquaintance with the purpose suffices. And if several things of one type are included in the sale, then if their units are not dissimilar — like measured and weighed items, and its sign is that they are presented as a sample — seeing one of them suffices, unless the rest are very inferior, in which case the buyer has the option. And if the units of those items are dissimilar — like cloths and animals — seeing each one is necessary. Nuts and eggs are also of this category according to al-Karkhi's statement. It would be appropriate that they be like wheat and barley because their units are similar. When this principle is established, we say: Looking at the top of the heap is sufficient because the description of the rest is known from it, since it is a measured item presented as a sample. Similarly, looking at the outer part of the cloth is of this category, because the rest is known from it, except when inside it there is something intended, like the place of embroidery. In a human, the face is what is intended; and in animals, the face and hindquarters are what is intended. So seeing the intended part is considered, and seeing other than it is not considered. Some have stipulated seeing the limbs, but the first is narrated from Abu Yusuf. In a sheep for meat, weighing is necessary because the intended meat is known by it. In a sheep for keeping, seeing the udder is necessary. In edible things, tasting is necessary because that is what gives knowledge of the purpose. Explanation of the case: If the buyer looks at the top of a heap of grain and does not look inside, or looks at the outer part of a folded cloth without opening it, or looks at a slave-girl's face, or looks at an animal's face and hindquarters, in these cases he will not have the option of inspection. The rule regarding establishment and dropping of the option of inspection is that the sold item is either a single thing or multiple things. If multiple things, then either their units are dissimilar or similar — meaning their units and individuals differ in value and price, or they are similar. The recognition of this is that if one of them is presented as a sample and specimen, then those items are of similar units; if not presented as a sample, then they are of dissimilar units. For example, items of the category of measured and weighed things are of similar units, because a small amount of grain from them is taken and presented as a sample. Animals and many cloths are of dissimilar units, because one animal among animals or one cloth among many cloths is not presented as a sample. In any case, sales become three types: 1. The sold item is a single thing. 2. The sold item is multiple things of similar units. 3. The sold item is multiple things of dissimilar units. If the first — i.e., the sold item is a single thing — then for dropping the option of inspection, seeing all of it is not required, because sometimes seeing all is impossible, as if the sold item is a male slave or a female slave, seeing all of their bodies would require seeing their private parts and their nakedness, which is forbidden by Shari'a. Thus it is established that seeing all of the sold item is impossible. Similarly, opening a folded and lain cloth would harm the seller because the fold of the entire cloth would break and ruin the cloth. Similarly, seeing every single grain of a heap is not possible. So when seeing all is impossible, seeing that much of the sold item which gives knowledge of the purpose suffices. And if the sold item is of the second type — i.e., multiple things of similar units, like measured and weighed things and similar countable items, and nuts and eggs according to the author's statement.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal explanation (sharh) of the rulings on khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) in Hanafi fiqh. The commentary first addresses when the buyer's disposition (tasarruf) before or after inspection nullifies the option. The key distinction is whether the disposition makes rescission impossible or creates a third-party right; if so, the option is lost even before inspection. But dispositions that do not have these effects, such as selling with a condition or gifting without possession, do not void the option before inspection because they are merely implied consent, and explicit consent before inspection does not void the option either. After inspection, however, any such disposition indicates consent and voids the option. The passage then presents the text of Quduri: looking only at the surface of a heap, the outer part of a folded cloth, the face of a slave-girl, or the face and hindquarters of an animal does not give the buyer the option of inspection because seeing the entire item is not required; it is sufficient to see that which indicates the purpose. The commentary elaborates this principle, dividing the sold item into three categories: single item, multiple items of similar units (like measured/weighed goods), and multiple items of dissimilar units (like cloths and animals). For a single item, partial inspection suffices if it indicates the whole. For similar items, seeing one representative sample suffices unless the rest are inferior. For dissimilar items, each must be inspected. Special rules apply: for a sheep for meat, weighing is needed; for a sheep for breeding, seeing the udder; for edible items, tasting. The authority weight of these rulings is from the Hanafi school, with some views attributed to Abu Yusuf and al-Karkhi.

Key Terms
خیار رویت (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection: the right of the buyer to rescind a sale after seeing the item if it does not meet expectations, when the item was purchased unseen.
دلالۂ رضاء (dalalat al-rida'): Implied consent; a disposition that indicates the buyer's approval without explicit verbal expression.
تصرف (tasarruf): Disposition; any legal act by the buyer that indicates ownership or control over the sold item, such as selling, gifting, or using.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer buys wheat unseen, then before inspection, sells it to another person with a condition that he can return it within three days (khiyar al-shart). Later he sees the wheat and finds it inferior. Does he have khiyar al-ru'yah against the original seller?
A) Yes, because he never inspected it.
B) No, because his sale is a disposition that creates a third-party right (the buyer's right under khiyar al-shart).
C) Yes, because the disposition was before inspection and did not make rescission impossible.
D) No, because he already sold it and cannot rescind.

Key Takeaway
Dispositions that make rescission impossible or create third-party rights void khiyar al-ru'yah even before inspection; otherwise, implied consent before inspection does not void the option.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
When Partial Inspection Suffices in Khiyar al-Ru'yah
Explains when partial inspection suffices and rules for different types of goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when partial inspection of a sold item suffices for khiyar al-ru'yah and classify goods as uniform or non-uniform accordingly.

Core Idea
For uniform measured items, seeing one sample suffices; for non-uniform items, each must be inspected individually.

When a buyer purchases an item unseen, he has the right to inspect it. But must he see every part? This lesson explains that for practical reasons, full inspection is not required. Instead, Hanafi law lays down clear rules: for a single item, seeing the part that indicates the whole suffices; for multiple items of the same type, one sample suffices if they are uniform; if not, each must be seen. Special rules apply for animals, cloth, and food.

Guided Path
1
Classify the sold item
Is the sale of a single item or multiple items? If multiple, are they of similar units (muttafiq al-ahad) or dissimilar (muttafiq al-ahad)? Similar items are those that can be sampled (measured, weighed, countable with little variation). Dissimilar items vary significantly (e.g., each garment is unique).
The text gives examples: wheat and barley are similar; cloths and animals are dissimilar.
2
Apply the inspection rule
For a single item: partial inspection suffices if it indicates the intended purpose (e.g., face of slave, face and hind of animal; surface of heap; outer of folded cloth). For multiple similar items: seeing one representative sample suffices unless the rest are inferior, in which case option arises. For multiple dissimilar items: each must be seen.
Exception: if inside the cloth has embroidery, the outer inspection is not enough.
3
Identify the specific rule for each commodity
For animals: face and hindquarters suffice for cows/horses; for a sheep for meat, weight matters; for a sheep for breeding, udder must be seen. For edibles: tasting is required.
The relied-upon view (from Abu Yusuf) is that seeing limbs is not necessary.
4
Apply to scenario
Given a case (e.g., buyer buys 10 bags of rice, sees one, the rest are of lower quality) determine if he has the option.

پوری صبیح کا دیکھنا متعذر ہے۔ اسی طرح تہہ کئے ہوئے اور لیٹے ہوئے تمان کو کھولنے سے بائع کو ضرر ہوگا کہ پورے تھان کی تہہ ٹوٹ کر تھان ہی خراب ہو جائے گا۔ اسی طرح ڈھیر کے ہر ہر دانہ کو دیکھنا مستعد رہے۔ پس جب پوری طبیع کا دیکھنا متعذر ہے تو طبی میں تنی چیز کا دیکھنا کافی ہوگا جس سے مقصود کا علم حاصل ہو جائے اور اگر مبیع دوسری قسم یعنی اشیاء متعدده متقارب الآحاد ہیں جیسے ملکیلی اور موزونی چیزیں اور عددی متقارب، اور اخروٹ اور مرغی کے انڈے مصنف کے بیان کے مطابق
They invalidate the option of inspection even after seeing. The proof is that when the disposition made by the buyer becomes impossible to rescind, or when the disposition makes another's right obligatory, then rescinding the sale becomes impossible. When rescinding the sale becomes impossible, the buyer's option of inspection also becomes void. And if the buyer made a disposition in the sale that does not establish another's right — for example, he bought something without seeing, then sold it with the condition of a three-day option, or gave it for appraisal without seeing, or gifted it but did not give possession to the donee — in these three cases, a disposition occurred from the buyer, but it was not a disposition that makes another's right obligatory. Therefore, such a disposition before inspection does not void the buyer's option of inspection, because this disposition is an implied indication of consent. And it has already passed that before seeing the commodity, explicit expression of consent does not void the option of inspection. So when explicit consent before inspection does not void the option, implied consent a fortiori does not void it. Yes, after inspection, this type of disposition will void the option of inspection, because in that case implied consent is found, and just as after seeing the commodity explicit consent drops the option, so too implied consent drops it. He looked at the surface of a heap of wheat, or at the outer part of a folded cloth, looked at the face of a slave-girl, and looked at the face and hindquarters of an animal — for him there is no option. He said: Whoever looks at the surface of a heap, or at the outer part of a cloth folded, or at the face of a slave-girl, or at the face and hindquarters of an animal, then there is no option for him. The principle in this matter is that seeing the entire sold item is not required because it is impossible; so seeing that which indicates knowledge of the intended purpose suffices. And if things are included in the sale, then if their units do not differ — like measured and weighed items, and their sign is that they are presented as a sample — seeing one of them suffices, unless the rest are inferior, in which case he has the option. If their units differ — like cloths and animals — seeing each one is necessary. Nuts and eggs are of this category according to what al-Karkhi mentioned, but it would be appropriate that they be like wheat and barley because they are similar. Once this is established, we say: looking at the face of the heap suffices, because the description of the rest is known from it, since it is a measured item presented as a sample. Similarly, looking at the outer part of the cloth is of this category, because the rest is known from it, unless inside it there is something intended, like the place of embroidery. The face is what is intended in a human, and the face and hindquarters in animals. So seeing the intended part is considered, and seeing other than it is not considered. Some have stipulated seeing the limbs, but the first is narrated from Abu Yusuf. In a sheep for meat, weighing is necessary because the intended meat is known by it. In a sheep for keeping, seeing the udder is necessary. In edible things, tasting is necessary because that is what gives knowledge of the intended purpose. Translation: Al-Quduri said: Whoever looks at the top of a heap, or at the upper part of a folded cloth, or at the face of a slave-girl, or at the face and hindquarters of an animal, then there is no option of inspection for him. The principle in this chapter is that seeing all of the sold item is not required because it is impossible. So seeing that much which indicates acquaintance with the purpose suffices. And if several things of one type are included in the sale, then if their units are not dissimilar — like measured and weighed items, and its sign is that they are presented as a sample — seeing one of them suffices, unless the rest are very inferior, in which case the buyer has the option. And if the units of those items are dissimilar — like cloths and animals — seeing each one is necessary. Nuts and eggs are also of this category according to al-Karkhi's statement. It would be appropriate that they be like wheat and barley because their units are similar. When this principle is established, we say: Looking at the top of the heap is sufficient because the description of the rest is known from it, since it is a measured item presented as a sample. Similarly, looking at the outer part of the cloth is of this category, because the rest is known from it, except when inside it there is something intended, like the place of embroidery. In a human, the face is what is intended; and in animals, the face and hindquarters are what is intended. So seeing the intended part is considered, and seeing other than it is not considered. Some have stipulated seeing the limbs, but the first is narrated from Abu Yusuf. In a sheep for meat, weighing is necessary because the intended meat is known by it. In a sheep for keeping, seeing the udder is necessary. In edible things, tasting is necessary because that is what gives knowledge of the purpose. Explanation of the case: If the buyer looks at the top of a heap of grain and does not look inside, or looks at the outer part of a folded cloth without opening it, or looks at a slave-girl's face, or looks at an animal's face and hindquarters, in these cases he will not have the option of inspection. The rule regarding establishment and dropping of the option of inspection is that the sold item is either a single thing or multiple things. If multiple things, then either their units are dissimilar or similar — meaning their units and individuals differ in value and price, or they are similar. The recognition of this is that if one of them is presented as a sample and specimen, then those items are of similar units; if not presented as a sample, then they are of dissimilar units. For example, items of the category of measured and weighed things are of similar units, because a small amount of grain from them is taken and presented as a sample. Animals and many cloths are of dissimilar units, because one animal among animals or one cloth among many cloths is not presented as a sample. In any case, sales become three types: 1. The sold item is a single thing. 2. The sold item is multiple things of similar units. 3. The sold item is multiple things of dissimilar units. If the first — i.e., the sold item is a single thing — then for dropping the option of inspection, seeing all of it is not required, because sometimes seeing all is impossible, as if the sold item is a male slave or a female slave, seeing all of their bodies would require seeing their private parts and their nakedness, which is forbidden by Shari'a. Thus it is established that seeing all of the sold item is impossible. Similarly, opening a folded and lain cloth would harm the seller because the fold of the entire cloth would break and ruin the cloth. Similarly, seeing every single grain of a heap is not possible. So when seeing all is impossible, seeing that much of the sold item which gives knowledge of the purpose suffices. And if the sold item is of the second type — i.e., multiple things of similar units, like measured and weighed things and similar countable items, and nuts and eggs according to the author's statement.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal explanation (sharh) of the rulings on khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) in Hanafi fiqh. The commentary first addresses when the buyer's disposition (tasarruf) before or after inspection nullifies the option. The key distinction is whether the disposition makes rescission impossible or creates a third-party right; if so, the option is lost even before inspection. But dispositions that do not have these effects, such as selling with a condition or gifting without possession, do not void the option before inspection because they are merely implied consent, and explicit consent before inspection does not void the option either. After inspection, however, any such disposition indicates consent and voids the option. The passage then presents the text of Quduri: looking only at the surface of a heap, the outer part of a folded cloth, the face of a slave-girl, or the face and hindquarters of an animal does not give the buyer the option of inspection because seeing the entire item is not required; it is sufficient to see that which indicates the purpose. The commentary elaborates this principle, dividing the sold item into three categories: single item, multiple items of similar units (like measured/weighed goods), and multiple items of dissimilar units (like cloths and animals). For a single item, partial inspection suffices if it indicates the whole. For similar items, seeing one representative sample suffices unless the rest are inferior. For dissimilar items, each must be inspected. Special rules apply: for a sheep for meat, weighing is needed; for a sheep for breeding, seeing the udder; for edible items, tasting. The authority weight of these rulings is from the Hanafi school, with some views attributed to Abu Yusuf and al-Karkhi.

Key Terms
صبرة (sabrah): A heap or pile of grain or similar items; looking at its surface suffices for inspection of the whole.
متفق الآحاد (muttafiq al-ahad): Items of similar units; uniformity where one sample represents the whole.
مختلف الآحاد (mukhtalif al-ahad): Items of dissimilar units; each unit may vary significantly.
نمونہ (namunah): Sample; a representative portion presented to show quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following items requires individual inspection for each unit according to the Hanafi rule?
A) Ten bushels of wheat
B) Three identical bolts of cloth from the same roll
C) Five lengths of cloth, each of different design and quality
D) A sack of potatoes

Key Takeaway
Partial inspection suffices when it indicates the intended purpose; for multiple items, the rule depends on uniformity — similar items require one sample, dissimilar require each inspection.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Rules of Khiyar al-Ru'yah: Classification and Inspection Requirements
Explaining khiyar al-ru'yah rules for single and multiple items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify sold items into single, mutaqarib al-ahad (similar units), and mutafa'it al-ahad (diverse units) and determine whether inspecting one item suffices to eliminate khiyar al-ru'yah.

Core Idea
Inspection of one sample suffices for similar goods; each must be inspected for diverse goods.

Khiyar al-ru'yah gives the buyer who purchased unseen goods the right to annul the sale after seeing them. However, the Hanafi school has detailed rules about what 'seeing' suffices to eliminate this option. This lesson explains the core classification of sold items — single, multiple similar, or multiple diverse — and how that determines the inspection required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of sold items
The passage states that when the sold item is a single thing, full inspection is not required; for multiple items, they are either similar (mutaqarib al-ahad) or diverse (mutafa'it al-ahad). Recognize these three types and their definitions.
Similar items are those where one can serve as a sample; diverse items cannot.
2
Apply the rule for single items
For a single item (e.g., a slave, a cloth, a pile of grain), inspecting the part that reveals the intended purpose suffices. The entire item need not be seen if doing so is impossible or harmful.
Examples: looking at the face of a slave, top of a cloth, top of a grain pile.
3
Apply the rule for multiple similar items
For multiple items that are similar (e.g., several sacks of grain, walnuts), inspecting one sample suffices to eliminate the option, unless the rest is much inferior than the sample.
Rest must be examined if significantly lower quality.
4
Apply the rule for multiple diverse items
For multiple items that are diverse (e.g., pieces of cloth, livestock), each individual item must be inspected separately because one cannot represent the other.
Examples: different cloth patterns, different animals.
5
Consider custom and intended part
The elimination of the option depends on what is customarily intended in the item. For humans, the face; for animals, face and hindquarters; for houses, the courtyard or outside was sufficient originally, but today entering rooms is necessary due to diversity.
Custom (urf) plays a role; the ruling can change with time.

ان کے افراد قریب قریب ہیں۔ جب یہ اصل ثابت ہو گئی تو ہم کہتے ہیں کہ ڈھیر کو اوپر سے دیکھنا کافی ہے کیونکہ باقی کا وصف اس سے معلوم ہو [؟] جاتا ہے اسلئے کہ یہ مکیلی چیز ہے جو نمونہ کے طور پر پیش کی جاتی جانوروں میں چہرہ اور سرین مقصود ہے۔ پس مقصود کا دیکھنا معتبر ہوگا۔ اور مقصود کے سوا کا دیکھنا معتبر نہ ہوگا اور بعض جانوروں کے ہاتھ پاؤں دیکھنا شرط کیا ہے۔ اور قول اول ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے اور گوشت کی چوپایہ کا چہرہ اور اس کے سرین دیکھ لیئے تو ان صورتوں میں اس کو خیار رویت حاصل نہ ہو گا۔ خیار رویت کے اثبات اور اسقاط کے سلسلہ میں ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ مبیع شی واحد ہے یا اشیاء متعددہ ہیں اگر اشیاء متعددہ بہر حال بیع کی تین قسمیں ہو گئیں۔ ا۔ مبیع شی ء واحد ہو۔
۲۔ مبیع اشیاء متعددہ متقارب الآحاد ہوں۔
۳۔ اشیاء متعددہ متفاوت الآحاد ہوں۔
اگر اول ہے یعنی مبیع شی ء واحد ہے تو خیار رویت ساقط کرنے کیلئے پوری بیع کا دیکھنا شرط نہیں ہے کیونکہ بسا اوقات پوری مبیع کا دیکھنا نا ممکن ہوتا ہے جیسا کہ مبیع اگر غلام یا باندی ہو تو ان کے پورے بدن کو دیکھنے کیلئے ان کی شرمگاہوں اور ان کے ستر عورت کا دیکھنا بھی ضروری ہوگا حالانکہ یہ شرعاً حرام ہے۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ پوری مبیع کا دیکھنا متعذر ہے۔ اسی طرح تہہ کئے ہوئے اور لیٹے ہوئے تھان کو کھولنے سے بائع کو ضرر ہوگا کہ پورے تھان کی تہہ ٹوٹ کر تھان ہی خراب ہو جائے گا۔ اسی طرح ڈھیر کے ہر ہر دانہ کو دیکھنا متعذر ہے۔ پس جب پوری مبیع کا دیکھنا متعذر ہے تو مبیع میں تنی چیز کا دیکھنا کافی ہوگا جس سے مقصود کا علم حاصل ہو جائے اور اگر مبیع دوسری قسم یعنی اشیاء متعددہ متقارب الآحاد ہیں جیسے مکیلی اور موزونی چیزیں اور عددی متقارب، اور اخروٹ اور مرغی کے انڈے مصنف کے بیان کے مطابق تو خیار رویت ساقط کرنے کیلئے ان میں سے ایک کا دیکھنا کافی ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ایک کو دیکھ کر عدم تفاوت کی وجہ سے باقی کے اوصاف بھی معلوم ہو جائیں گے۔ ہاں اگر باقی مبیع، دیکھی ہوئی بانگی اور نمونہ سے بہت گھٹیا ہو تو اس صورت میں مشتری کو خیار رویت حاصل رہے گا۔ اور اگر مبیع تیسری قسم یعنی اشیاء متعدد و متفاوت الآحاد ہوں جیسے کپڑے کے تھان، چوپائے اور امام کرخی کے بیان کے مطابق اخروٹ اور مرغی کے انڈے تو ان اشیاء متعددہ میں سے ہر ایک کا دیکھنا ضروری ہے۔ کیونکہ افراد و احاد کے متفاوت ہونے کی وجہ سے ایک فرد کو دیکھ کر باقی کے اوصاف معلوم نہیں ہو سکتے۔ اسلئے ہر ہر فرد کا دیکھنا ضروری ہوگا۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس ضابطہ کے ثابت ہو جانے کے بعد ہم کہتے ہیں کہ اناج کے ڈھیر کو اوپر سے دیکھ لینا کافی ہے یعنی مشتری نے اگر اناج کے ڈھیر کو اوپر سے دیکھ کر خرید لیا تو اس کے واسطے خیار رویت ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اناج مکیلی چیز ہے نمونہ اور بانگی کے طور پر پیش کیا جاتا ہے۔ اسلئے ڈھیر کے اوپر کے حصہ کو دیکھنے سے باقی اناج کا وصف بھی معلوم ہو جائے گا۔ اسی طرح تھان کو اوپر سے دیکھ لینا کافی ہے جس سے باقی کا وصف معلوم ہو جاتا ہے۔ لیکن اگر تھان کی تہہ کے اندر ایسی چیز ہو جو مشتری کا مقصود ہے۔ مثلاً مشتری نے بیل بوٹے اور نقش و نگار کی وجہ سے تھان خریدا ہے تو ایسی صورت میں تھان کے اوپر سے دیکھ لینا کافی نہ ہوگا۔ بلکہ اندر سے دیکھنا بھی ضروری ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ آدمی کے اندر چہرہ مقصود ہے اور باقی تمام اعضاء اس کے تابع ہیں۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ دو غلاموں کے تمام اعضاء میں اگر یکسانیت ہو اور دونوں کے چہرے متفاوت ہوں تو ان کی قیمتیں متفاوت ہوتی ہیں۔ پس اگر کسی نے غلام یا باندی کا چہرہ دیکھ کر اس کو خرید لیا تو مشتری کو خیار رویت حاصل نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر اس کے تمام اعضاء کو دیکھا مگر چہرہ نہیں دیکھا تو مشتری کا خیار رویت باقی رہے گا۔ اور جانوروں کے اندر چونکہ چہرہ اور چوتڑ دونوں مقصود ہیں۔ اسلئے خیار ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ حاصل یہ کہ خیار رویت ساقط کرنے میں مقصود کی وی معتبر ہے غیر مقصود کی وی معتبر نہیں۔ بعض مشائخ کا خیال ہے کہ جانوروں کے اندر چہرہ اور چوتڑ کے علاوہ اس کے ہاتھ پاؤں کا دیکھنا بھی شرط ہے۔ کیونکہ جانوروں میں ہاتھ پاؤں بھی مقصود ہوتے ہیں۔ قول اول ( یعنی چہرہ اور چوتڑ کا دیکھنا کافی ہے) حضرت امام ابو یوسف کی روایت ہے۔ اور اگر بکری گوشت کی وجہ سے ذبح کرنے کیلئے خریدی گئی تو اس کو ہاتھ سے ٹول کر دیکھنا ضروری ہے کیونکہ گوشت جو اصلی مقصود ہے اسی طرح پہچانا جاتا ہے کہ وہ چربی دار موٹا تازہ ہے یا بالکل مریل اور بیکار ہے اور اگر بکری افزائش نسل اور دودھ کیلئے خریدی گئی ہے تو اس کے تھنوں کا دیکھنا ضروری ہے کیونکہ اس میں تھن ہی مقصود ہیں۔ اور جو چیز کھائی جاتی ہے اس میں چکھنا ضروری ہے۔ کیونکہ اس میں جو مقصود ہے وہ چکھنے سے معلوم ہوتا ہے۔
گھر کے صحن کو دیکھنے سے خیار رویت ختم ہو جاتا ہے، اگر چہ کمروں کو نہ دیکھا ہو، اسی طرح گھر کو باہر سے یا باغ کو باہر سے دیکھ لیا تو خیار رویت نہیں ہے
قال وان راى صحن الدار فلاخيار له وان لم يشاهد بيوتها و كذالک اذا راى خارج الدار اورای اشجار البستان من خارج و عند زفر لابد من دخول داخل البيوت والاصح ان جواب الكتاب على وفاق عادتهم فى الابنية فان دورهم لم تكن متفاوتة يومئذ فاما اليوم فلا بد من الدخول فى داخل الدار للتفاوت والنظر
Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, Volume 8. If [items] are of one kind, looking at one of them suffices. But if the rest are much inferior to what was seen, then the buyer has the option of inspection. And if the individuals of these items are diverse, like cloth and livestock, then looking at each one is necessary. Walnuts and chicken eggs, according to Karkhi's statement, are also of this type. It would be appropriate if they were like wheat and barley because their individuals are close. When this principle is established, we say that looking at the pile from above is sufficient because the description of the rest is known from it, as it is a measured item presented as a sample. Similarly, looking at the cloth from above is of this type, because the description of the rest is known from it, unless there is something inside that is intended, like the place of embroidery, and in a human, the face is intended, and in animals, the face and hindquarters are intended. So looking at what is intended is considered, and looking at other than what is intended is not considered. For some animals, looking at the hands and feet is stipulated. The first statement is narrated from Abu Yusuf. For a goat for meat, weighing is necessary because meat, which is intended, is known by it. For a goat for breeding, looking at the udders is necessary. For food items, tasting is necessary, because tasting is what informs about the intended [quality]. Explanation of the case: If the buyer looked at the pile of grain from above and did not look at the grain inside, or looked at the folded cloth from above without unfolding it, or looked at the face of a slave girl, or looked at the face and hindquarters of an animal, then in these cases the option of inspection is not established. The rule for establishing or eliminating the option of inspection is: Is the sold item a single thing or multiple things? If multiple, are they diverse in individuals or similar in individuals? That is, are their individuals diverse in value and price or similar? The way to recognize this is that if one of them is presented as a sample and specimen, then they are similar in individuals; if not presented as a sample, then they are diverse in individuals. For example, things from the categories of measured and weighed items are similar in individuals because a small amount of grain is taken and presented as a sample. Livestock and many pieces of cloth are diverse in individuals, because one animal out of many or one piece of cloth out of many is not presented as a sample. In any case, there are three types of sale: 1. The sold item is a single thing. 2. The sold items are multiple but similar in individuals. 3. The sold items are multiple and diverse in individuals. If the first, i.e., the sold item is single, then to eliminate the option of inspection it is not required to see the entire sold item, because sometimes it is impossible to see the entire sold item, like if the sold item is a male or female slave, then to see their entire body would require seeing their private parts and the woman's genitals, which is forbidden by Sharia. Thus it is established that seeing the entire sold item is impossible. Similarly, unfolding a folded and laid-out cloth would harm the seller because the fold of the entire cloth would break and the cloth itself would be damaged. Similarly, seeing every single grain of a pile is impossible. So when seeing the entire sold item is impossible, seeing something in the sold item that gives knowledge of the intended [part] suffices. If the sold item is of the second type, i.e., multiple items similar in individuals, like measured and weighed items and similar countable items, and walnuts and chicken eggs according to the author's statement, then to eliminate the option of inspection, seeing one of them suffices, because by seeing one, due to lack of diversity, the descriptions of the rest are also known. However, if the rest of the sold item is much inferior to what was seen as a sample and specimen, then the buyer retains the option of inspection. If the sold item is of the third type, i.e., multiple and diverse items, like pieces of cloth, livestock, and according to Imam Karkhi walnuts and chicken eggs, then seeing each of these multiple items is necessary, because due to the diversity of individuals, the descriptions of the rest cannot be known by seeing one individual. Therefore, seeing every individual is necessary. The author of Hidayah says: When this rule is established, we say that looking at the pile of grain from above is sufficient, i.e., if the buyer looked at the pile of grain from above and bought it, then the option of inspection is not established for him, because grain is a measured item presented as a sample and specimen. Therefore, looking at the top part of the pile gives knowledge of the description of the rest of the grain. Similarly, looking at the cloth from above suffices, because the description of the rest is known from it. But if there is something inside the cloth that is the buyer's intended purpose, for example, the buyer bought the cloth on account of its embroidery and design, then in such a case, looking at the cloth from above is not sufficient; rather, looking inside is also necessary. The author of Hidayah says: In a human, the face is the intended part, and all other limbs are subordinate. This is why if two slaves have all limbs identical but faces are different, their prices are different. So if someone looked at the face of a male or female slave and bought him/her, the buyer does not have the option of inspection. If he looked at all his/her limbs but not the face, the buyer retains the option of inspection. In animals, since both the face and hindquarters are intended, the option is eliminated. The conclusion is that to eliminate the option of inspection, looking at what is intended is considered; looking at what is not intended is not considered. Some mashayikh think that for animals, in addition to the face and hindquarters, looking at the hands and feet is also required, because in animals the hands and feet are also intended. The first statement (that looking at the face and hindquarters suffices) is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf. If a goat is bought for slaughter for meat, then it is necessary to weigh it by hand because meat, which is the primary intended thing, is known in this way whether it is fatty, plump, fresh, or very lean and worthless. If a goat is bought for breeding and milk, then looking at its udders is necessary because the udders are what is intended. For something that is eaten, tasting is necessary, because what is intended in it is known by tasting. Looking at the courtyard of a house eliminates the option of inspection, even if the rooms were not seen. Similarly, looking at the house from outside or the garden from outside, there is no option of inspection. He said: If he saw the courtyard of the house, he has no option even if he did not see its rooms. Similarly, if he saw the outside of the house, or saw the trees of the garden from outside. According to Zufar, it is necessary to enter inside the rooms. The correct view is that the response of the Book is in accordance with their custom regarding buildings, for their houses were not diverse in those days. But today, one must enter inside the house due to diversity and consideration.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah on the chapter of sales, specifically explaining the rules of khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) as derived from Al-Hidayah. The commentary first establishes a general principle based on whether the sold item is a single object or multiple objects, and if multiple, whether they are similar (mutaqarib al-ahad) or diverse (mutafa'it al-ahad). For a single item, full inspection is not required if impossible or harmful; instead, inspecting the part that indicates the intended purpose suffices. For multiple similar items (e.g., grain, weighed goods), inspecting one sample suffices unless the rest is much inferior. For multiple diverse items (e.g., cloth pieces, livestock), each must be inspected individually. Then the commentary applies these rules to specific cases: looking at the top of a grain pile or folded cloth is enough because they are treated as samples. However, if the intended feature (e.g., embroidery) is inside, then internal inspection is needed. For humans, the face is the intended part; for animals, both face and hindquarters are intended. There is a difference of opinion: some mashayikh require seeing hands and feet of animals as well, but the relied-upon view (from Abu Yusuf) is that face and hindquarters suffice. For goats bought for meat, weighing is necessary; for breeding, looking at udders; for food, tasting. Finally, the commentary addresses looking at a house: seeing the courtyard or outside suffices, but Zufar says entering rooms is necessary. The correct view is that the ruling in Al-Hidayah was based on the custom of those days when houses were similar; today, due to diversity, entering is required. This shows the principle that khiyar al-ru'yah is linked to what is customary and intended.

Key Terms
خیار الرؤیہ (khiyar al-ru'yah): The buyer's option to rescind or confirm a sale after inspecting goods that were not seen at the time of contract.
متقارب الآحاد (mutaqarib al-ahad): Items whose individual units are similar in value and description, so one sample represents the whole.
متفاوت الآحاد (mutafa'it al-ahad): Items whose individual units are diverse in value and description, so each must be inspected separately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases 5 cows and 5 sacks of wheat. If he inspects only one cow and one sack of wheat, for which items does the option of inspection remain?
A) Both cows and wheat
B) Only the cows
C) Only the wheat
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The key to khiyar al-ru'yah is classifying goods: single items require seeing the intended part; multiple similar items require one sample unless inferior; multiple diverse items require inspecting each individually.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah: Homogeneous vs Heterogeneous Goods
States when seeing one item suffices to drop the option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between homogeneous and heterogeneous goods in khiyar al-ru'yah and state when seeing one item suffices to drop the option.

Core Idea
Seeing one item drops khiyar al-ru'yah only for homogeneous goods; heterogeneous ones require each to be seen.

The option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) protects the buyer who purchases an unseen item. When multiple items are sold together, the question arises: does seeing one suffice? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling, which distinguishes between homogeneous and heterogeneous goods and also addresses the case of an inferior sample.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the goods
Identify whether the sold items are homogeneous (identical in type and quality) or heterogeneous (individually different).
Homogeneous: e.g., identical apples. Heterogeneous: e.g., different cloth pieces, animals.
2
Apply the base rule for homogeneous goods
If goods are homogeneous and one item is seen, the khiyar al-ru'yah drops for all, because knowing one reveals the attributes of the rest.
Exception: if the remaining items are much inferior to the seen sample, the option remains.
3
Apply the rule for heterogeneous goods
If goods are heterogeneous (e.g., different cloth pieces, animals, and according to Imam al-Karkhi, walnuts and chicken eggs), each item must be seen individually for the option to drop.
The attributes of one cannot be inferred for the others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تو خیار رویت ساقط کرنے کیلئے ان میں سے ایک کا دیکھنا کافی ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ایک کو دیکھ کر عدم تفاوت کی وجہ سے باقی کے اوصاف بھی معلوم ہو جائیں گے۔ ہاں اگر باقی مبیع، دیکھی ہوئی بانگی اور نمونہ سے بہت گھٹیا ہو تو اس صورت میں مشتری کو خیار رویت حاصل رہے گا۔ اور اگر مبیع تیسری قسم یعنی اشیاء متعدد و متفاوت الآحاد ہوں جیسے کپڑے کے تھان، چوپائے اور امام کرخی کے بیان کے مطابق اخروٹ اور مرغی کے انڈے تو ان اشیاء متعددہ میں سے ہر ایک کا دیکھنا ضروری ہے۔ کیونکہ افراد و احاد کے متفاوت ہونے کی وجہ سے ایک فرد کو دیکھ کر باقی کے اوصاف معلوم نہیں ہو سکتے۔ اسلئے ہر ہر فرد کا دیکھنا ضروری ہوگا۔
To drop the option of inspection, seeing one of them will be sufficient. Because by seeing one, due to the lack of difference, the attributes of the rest will also be known. Yes, if the remaining item is much inferior to the seen sample or specimen, then in that case the buyer will retain the option of inspection. If the sold items are of the third type, i.e., multiple and individually different, like cloth pieces, quadrupeds, and according to Imam al-Karkhi, walnuts and chicken eggs, then it is necessary to see each of these multiple items. Because due to the individuals being different, by seeing one individual the attributes of the rest cannot be known. Therefore, seeing every individual is necessary.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining how the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) applies when multiple items are sold in a single sale. The author distinguishes three categories: (1) If the items are homogeneous and one is seen, the option drops because knowing one reveals the attributes of all. However, if the remaining items are significantly inferior to the sample, the option remains. (2) If the items are of the third type—multiple and individually different—like different cloth pieces, animals, or even nuts and eggs according to Imam al-Karkhi, then each item must be seen individually because one cannot infer the attributes of others from a single one. The ruling is based on the principle that khiyar al-ru'yah is established to protect the buyer from deception; if attributes are unknown, the option remains.

Key Terms
خیار رویت (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection; the right of the buyer to rescind the sale when the item is seen after contract and found unsatisfactory.
بانگی (bangī): A sample or specimen shown to the buyer before sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does seeing one item from a group of sold items NOT suffice to drop the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah)?
A) When the items are homogeneous and the seen item is a fair sample.
B) When the items are individually different (heterogeneous).
C) When the buyer explicitly waives the option.
D) When the seller guarantees the quality.

Key Takeaway
The khiyar al-ru'yah drops upon seeing one item only if the goods are homogeneous and of comparable quality; heterogeneous goods require individual inspection.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah for Grain Heap and Cloth
Explains when seeing the top of a grain heap or cloth suffices to drop the option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when seeing the top of a grain heap or cloth suffices to drop khiyar al-ru'yah and identify the exception for inner patterns.

Core Idea
Seeing the top of a grain heap or cloth suffices to drop khiyar al-ru'yah because it reveals the whole, unless the buyer’s intent is the inner design.

This passage from al-Hidayah applies the rule of khiyar al-ru'yah to specific items. Al-Marghinani explains that for measured goods like grain, seeing the top is sufficient. The same applies to cloth, but with an important exception when the inner pattern is the buyer's purpose. Understanding this helps determine when the option of inspection is validly dropped in sales of bulk or layered items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule of khiyar al-ru'yah
Recall that khiyar al-ru'yah gives the buyer the option to rescind after seeing an unseen item. The option drops when the buyer sees the item and is satisfied with its attributes.
This rule is established earlier in al-Hidayah.
2
Apply to grain heap
For makili items like grain, seeing the top is sufficient because the top serves as a sample revealing the attributes of the whole heap.
Grain is homogeneous; a sample represents the whole.
3
Apply to cloth
For cloth, seeing the outer side typically suffices. However, if the buyer's intention is the inner pattern (e.g., embroidery), then outer viewing is insufficient.
The exception hinges on the buyer's specific purpose.
4
Understand the legal consequence
If the seen part adequately reveals the attributes, the sale is binding and khiyar drops. If not, the option remains or a defect may trigger khiyar al-'ayb.
The key is whether the seen part reveals the unseen attribute.

صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس ضابطہ کے ثابت ہو جانے کے بعد ہم کہتے ہیں کہ اناج کے ڈھیر کو اوپر سے دیکھ لینا کافی ہے یعنی مشتری نے اگر اناج کے ڈھیر کو اوپر سے دیکھ کر خرید لیا تو اس کے واسطے خیار رویت ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اناج مکیلی چیز ہے نمونہ اور بانگی کے طور پر پیش کیا جاتا ہے۔ اسلئے ڈھیر کے اوپر کے حصہ کو دیکھنے سے باقی اناج کا وصف بھی معلوم ہو جائے گا۔ اسی طرح تھان کو اوپر سے دیکھ لینا کافی ہے جس سے باقی کا وصف معلوم ہو جاتا ہے۔ لیکن اگر تھان کی تہہ کے اندر ایسی چیز ہو جو مشتری کا مقصود ہے۔ مثلاً مشتری نے بیل بوٹے اور نقش و نگار کی وجہ سے تھان خریدا ہے تو ایسی صورت میں تھان کے اوپر سے دیکھ لینا کافی نہ ہوگا۔ بلکہ اندر سے دیکھنا بھی ضروری ہے۔
The author of al-Hidayah says: After this rule is established, we say that seeing a grain heap from the top is sufficient, i.e., if the buyer saw the grain heap from the top and bought it, the option of inspection will not be established for him. Because grain is a measured (makili) thing and it is presented as a sample and specimen. Therefore, by seeing the upper part of the heap, the attribute of the remaining grain will also be known. Similarly, seeing a cloth from the top is sufficient, and from that the attribute of the rest is known. But if there is something in the folds of the cloth that is the buyer's intent, for example, the buyer bought the cloth for its patterns and embroidery, then in that case seeing the top of the cloth is not sufficient; rather, seeing the inside is also necessary.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on khiyar al-ru'yah by applying the rule to specific items. The author of al-Hidayah (al-Marghinani) explains that for a grain heap, seeing the top is enough because grain is a measured commodity where the sample (bangī) represents the whole. The same applies to a cloth; seeing the outer part generally suffices. However, if the buyer's purpose is specifically the inner design (e.g., embroidery), then he must inspect the inside as well, or the option remains. The ruling is based on the principle that khiyar al-ru'yah is about the buyer's knowledge of the attributes; if the outer part reveals the inner attributes, the option drops; otherwise not.

Key Terms
مکیلی (makīlī): Something that is sold by measure (kayl), e.g., grain.
تھان (thān): A piece of cloth, typically a bolt or roll.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a buyer purchases a grain heap after seeing only the top, is the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) dropped?
A) Yes, because grain is a measured commodity and the top represents the whole.
B) No, because the entire heap must be seen.
C) Only if the heap is small.
D) Only if the buyer explicitly waives the option.

Key Takeaway
The outer appearance of a homogeneous item suffices to drop khiyar al-ru'yah, but the rule adapts to the buyer's specific intent.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah and the Principle of Maqsud
Explains which body parts are primary for inspection of humans and animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary intended part (maqsud) that must be inspected to drop khiyar al-ru'yah in slaves, animals, and edibles, and apply the Hanafi rulings to purchase scenarios.

Core Idea
The option of inspection drops only when the buyer sees the primary intended part (maqsud) – face for humans, face and hindquarters for animals.

This passage from al-Hidayah explains a key principle in khiyar al-ru'yah: the option drops only when the buyer has seen the part of the item that is its primary intended attribute (maqsud). The author then specifies the maqsud for different categories – humans, animals, and edibles – and introduces an intra-Hanafi difference about animals. Understanding this principle prevents disputes over whether the option remains after partial inspection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central principle
Recognize that khiyar al-ru'yah drops only when the maqsud (primary intended part) has been seen. Non-primary parts are subsidiary and their inspection does not drop the option.
This is a qa'ida: 'I'tibar al-maqsud fi isqat al-khiyar.'
2
Apply to humans
For a human slave, the face is the maqsud. Seeing the face alone drops the option; seeing all other parts but not the face leaves the option intact.
Evidence: the price of two identical slaves differs if faces differ.
3
Apply to animals
For animals, both face and hindquarters are maqsud. Seeing both is required to drop the option. Note the ikhtilaf: some Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) require also hands and feet.
Mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view that face and hindquarters suffice.
4
Consider purpose-specific inspection
If the buyer's purpose is specific (e.g., goat for meat vs. milk), the maqsud shifts: for meat, palpation; for milk, udders; for edibles, tasting.
The rule: the maqsud is defined by what the buyer primarily intends.
5
Synthesize the rulings
Combine the general rule with purpose: determine the category, then apply the appropriate inspection requirement to decide whether khiyar al-ru'yah drops.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ آدمی کے اندر چہرہ مقصور ہے اور باقی تمام اعضاء اس کے تابع ہیں۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ دو غلاموں کے تمام اعضاء میں اگر یکسانیت ہو اور دونوں کے چہرے متفاوت ہوں تو ان کی قیمتیں متفاوت ہوتی ہیں۔ پس اگر کسی نے غلام یا باندی کا چہرہ دیکھ کر اس کو خرید لیا تو مشتری کو خیار رویت حاصل نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر اس کے تمام اعضاء کو دیکھا مگر چہرہ نہیں دیکھا تو مشتری کا خیار رویت باقی رہے گا۔ اور جانوروں کے اندر چونکہ چہرہ اور چوتڑ دونوں مقصود ہیں۔ اسلئے خیار ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ حاصل یہ کہ خیار رویت ساقط کرنے میں مقصود کی رویت معتبر ہے غیر مقصود کی رویت معتبر نہیں۔ بی شائخ کا خیال ہے کہ جانوروں کے اندر چہرہ اور چوتڑ کے علاوہ اس کے ہاتھ پاؤں کا دیکھنا بھی شرط ہے۔ کیونکہ جانوروں میں ہاتھ پاؤں بھی مقصود ہوتے ہیں۔ قول اول (یعنی چہرہ اور چوتڑ کا دیکھنا کافی ہے) حضرت امام ابو یوسف کی روایت ہے۔ اور اگر بکری گوشت کی وجہ سے ذبح کرنے کیلئے خریدی گئی تو اس کو ہاتھ سے ٹول کر دیکھنا ضروری ہے کیونکہ گوشت جو اصلی مقصود ہے اسی طرح پہچانا جاتا ہے کہ وہ چربی دار موٹا تازہ ہے یا بالکل مریل اور بریکار ہے اور اگر بکری افزائش نسل اور دودھ کیلئے خریدی گئی ہے تو اس کے تھنوں کا دیکھنا ضروری ہے کیونکہ اس میں تھن ہی مقصود ہیں۔ اور جو چیز کھائی جاتی ہے اس میں چکھنا ضروری ہے۔ کیونکہ اس میں جو مقصود ہے وہ چکھنے سے معلوم ہوتا ہے۔
The author of al-Hidayah says: In a human, the face is the primary object (maqsud), and all other parts are subsidiary. This is why if two slaves are identical in all limbs but their faces are different, their prices differ. So if someone bought a male or female slave after seeing the face, he will have no option of inspection. If he saw all other limbs but not the face, his option remains. In animals, since both the face and the hindquarters are primary, the option drops. The conclusion: in dropping the option of inspection, seeing the primary object is considered; seeing what is not primary is not considered. Some mashayikh hold that for animals, besides face and hindquarters, seeing the hands and feet is also a condition, because hands and feet are also intended. The first opinion (that seeing face and hindquarters is sufficient) is a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf. If a goat is bought for slaughter for meat, it is necessary to palpate it by hand because meat, the real object, is known that way—whether it is fatty, fleshy, fresh, or lean and tough. If the goat is bought for breeding and milk, then seeing its udders is necessary because the udders are the object. And for something that is eaten, tasting is necessary because the object is known by tasting.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on khiyar al-ru'yah for different classes of living beings and comestibles. For humans (slaves), the face is the primary determinant of value; thus seeing the face alone drops the option. For animals, both face and hindquarters are primary; seeing both drops the option. There is an ikhtilaf: some scholars (mashayikh) require seeing the limbs as well, but the relied-upon narration from Imam Abu Yusuf is that face and hindquarters suffice. The passage then addresses specific purposes: if a goat is bought for meat, palpation (tolna) is necessary to assess meat quality; if for breeding or milk, udders must be seen. For edible items, tasting is required because the quality is known by taste. These rulings are based on what the buyer primarily intends (maqsud) in the transaction.

Key Terms
مقصود (maqsūd): The primary intended attribute or part of the item that determines its value for the buyer.
چوتڑ (chūṭaṛ): Hindquarters or rear end of an animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone buys a goat for milk production, which part must be seen to drop the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah)?
A) The face only.
B) The face and the hindquarters.
C) The udders.
D) The entire body.

Key Takeaway
The option of inspection drops only when the buyer has seen the specific primary part (maqsud) of the item, which varies by species and purpose.
